Authors: Christian Fruerhøj 
Title: Parameterised design of floaters for offshore wind turbines 
 
DTU Wind Energy-M-0151 
July 2017 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
 
DTU Wind Energy is a department of the Technical University of Denmark 
with a unique integration of research, education, innovation and public/private 
sector consulting in the field of wind energy. Our activities develop new 
opportunities and technology for the global and Danish exploitation of wind 
energy. Research focuses on key technical-scientific fields, which are central 
for the development, innovation and use of wind energy and provides the 
basis for advanced education at the education. 
 
We have more than 240 staff members of which approximately 60 are PhD 
students. Research is conducted within nine research programmes organized 
into three main topics: Wind energy systems, Wind turbine technology and 
Basics for wind energy.

==PAGE:3==
Abstract
This work presents the numerical results from parametric design investigations concern-
ing ﬂoating wind turbine substructures. Two concepts are investigated; namely a concrete
semi-submersible ﬂoater termed TripleSpar, and a new concept developed with indus-
trialisation in mind, which features a novel external suspended ballasted keel, termed
TetraSpar.
Results were produced using a quick linear frequency-domain numerical model,
QuLAF, developed in Matlab. This allowed for a fast simulation time when investi-
gating several designs, while still capturing the most important phenomena. The model
takes input from WAMIT and FAST coupled with viscous eﬀects from the Morison drag
term, and features a catenary mooring system linearised around the ﬂoater’s equilibrium
position.
A sensitivity analysis on varying a set of input parameters for the TripleSpar ﬂoater
was conducted, and possible design limits were quantiﬁed. The design space was then
further explored by means of analysing two signiﬁcantly diﬀerent TripleSpar conﬁgura-
tions. Next, the model was adapted to consider the new novel concept of the TetraSpar
featuring a suspended keel. Two diﬀerent conﬁgurations of this concept were investi-
gated, when varying the ballast depth and mass. Finally, a TetraSpar design compliant
with the 50th year sea state at Buchan Deep was proposed.
The sensitivity analysis and associated investigations made it possible to impose de-
sign limits on ﬂoater parameters, in order for it to withstand especially static wind forcing
at two times rated thrust. Thus underlining the necessity to ensure suﬃcient pitch stiﬀ-
ness at all times. For the two diﬀerent TripleSpar conﬁgurations a spiral of ever increasing
ﬂoater dimensions was encountered and the design was aborted. Concerning the new novel
ﬂoater it was found that using a spar conﬁguration with only a central transition piece
piercing the waterline, resulted in excellent response characteristics, even for very severe
sea states such as the 50th year sea state at Buchan Deep. It was not possible to produce
a satisfactory semisub conﬁguration, due to the combination of its light weight and high
hydrostatic stiﬀness which resulted in high natural frequencies entailing wave excitation.
The semisub was contrary to the spar severely aﬀected by the chosen sea state.

Preface and Acknowledgements
This Master of Science thesis was carried out at the Department of Wind Energy, Sec-
tion for Fluid Mechanics, Technical University of Denmark, in partial fulﬁlment of the
requirements for obtaining the degree Master of Science in Civil Engineering. The project
is credited to 30 ECTS points and was carried out in the period from January to July
2017. The thesis supervisors were very clear and speciﬁc when suggesting the project
topic and were also very much aware of which subjects were of interest to the author. A
match between interests of the author and a possible project thus swiftly ensued. Minor
adjustments to the project have been carried out during the project period in agreement
between the author and the supervisors.
I would like to thank my main supervisor, associate professor Henrik Bredmose, for
help and support during the project. Henrik’s enthusiasm of the oﬀshore wind industry
and pleasant demeanour at every meeting has been really inspiring. An immense thanks
must also be sent to my co-supervisor, postdoc Michael Borg, who has been an indis-
pensable help to me. A thanks should also be given to co-supervisor, honorary professor
Henrik Stiesdal, for assistance with establishing a credible cost function. Lastly, a special
thanks to PhD candidate Antonio M. P. Jurado, the author of the original QuLAF code,
who gave a thorough introduction to the code and provided comprehensive answers to
arising questions concerning the QuLAF code.
As last-mentions are my oﬃce mate Poul Winther Sall, who worked on a project in-
volving modal identiﬁcation of damping for ﬂoating wind turbines, and my good friend
Rune Okholm Just. Poul, thank you for the good discussions we shared at our oﬃce and
for your always positive attitude. Rune, thank you for your willingness to proofread this
thesis.
Kongens Lyngby, 14th July 2017
Christian Fruerhøj

Chapter1
Introduction
As the eﬀects of greenhouse gas emissions become ever more evident with the rising
global temperatures, and the fact that energy demands will inevitably increase in future,
implies that zero emission energy production methods must be developed on an extensive
commercial scale. [1] predicts that global energy consumption will increase by 48 % from
the consumption in 2012 to 2040. Oﬀshore wind energy has for well over a decade now
established itself as a viable source of renewable energy, with measures constantly being
taken in order to bring down the cost further and to increase eﬃciency.
Oﬀshore wind energy is currently characterised by wind turbines situated oﬀshore
on various types of bottom-ﬁxed support structures. The turbines harness the power
of the wind by generating torque from the turning of the blades. This torque is used
to rotate a shaft connected to a generator within the nacelle thus producing electricity.
The amount of oﬀshore wind turbines installed is constantly expanding. Especially the
United Kingdom, Germany, Denmark and the Netherlands are at the forefront of this
expansion, but also non-OECD countries such as China are gearing up on oﬀshore wind,
as depicted on Figure 1.1.
Figure 1.1: Global oﬀshore wind market share at the end of 2016 and forecast by 2020 [2].
1
==PAGE:26==
2
Chapter 1. Introduction
The cumulative amount of installed oﬀshore wind capacity has seen a remarkable increase
in the latest years, and with new projects in the pipeline, this tendency will increase even
further in future, as depicted on Figure 1.2. With the ongoing research in oﬀshore ﬂoating
wind, and the resulting unlocking of new potential deep-water areas such as the coasts
oﬀJapan, Portugal and the United States, the increase in oﬀshore wind capacity might
increase much more past 2020.
The motivation for installing wind turbines on ﬂoating substructures is the potential
to harness the generally stronger and more consistent winds occurring farther out at sea,
while at the same time minimising visual pollution, disruption of bird migration patterns
and interference with shipping lanes. It could be hypothesised that the increased amount
of power generated by installing the wind turbines on ﬂoating substructures farther out
at sea, will tip the scale in favour of ﬂoating wind as a more long-term cost-eﬃcient
way of generating renewable energy, compared to present bottom-ﬁxed foundations.
Nevertheless, while technology is gradually maturing, extensive research is still required
to demonstrate the economics of full size ﬂoating wind farms.
Figure 1.2: Annual and cumulative installed oﬀshore wind capacity [2].
There is no doubt that oﬀshore ﬂoating wind resources are abundant, and for instance
oﬀthe west coast of the United States where a steep continental slope is present, ﬂoating
wind could be the only technically and economically feasible oﬀshore wind solution. The
U.S. State of California passed in 2015 a legislation requiring that half the state’s energy
consumption in 2030 must be met with renewable energy sources [3]. A recent report [4]
states that in water depths less than 1,000 meters oﬀthe coast of California, and where
==PAGE:27==
1.1. Oﬀshore Wind Foundation Types
3
suﬃciently high wind speeds occur, 112 GW of technical oﬀshore wind resource potential
is located. This is stated to correspond to 392 TWh/year of potential energy, which is
about 1.5 times California’s energy consumption in the year 2014. 96 % of these avail-
able oﬀshore wind resources are located in water depths exceeding 60 m, making ﬁxed
foundations non-feasible. Floating wind is thus an essential technology to keep in mind,
should California focus on oﬀshore wind as a means for meeting its 2030 goal.
At the moment there is a need to drive ﬂoating wind costs down in order for the tech-
nology to really kick-oﬀ, and compete with bottom-ﬁxed oﬀshore wind on a commercial
scale. Some of this cost-reduction must be expected to come from the general cost devel-
opment within the oﬀshore wind sector, as processes are further streamlined and even
more experience in design and manufacturing is acquired. However, clever solutions in
relation to ﬂoating substructure design and manufacturing should be a point of focus as
well.
1.1
Oﬀshore Wind Foundation Types
As of today virtually every oﬀshore wind farm is situated on depths suitable for bottom-
ﬁxed foundations. A noteworthy exception being the Hywind Scotland Pilot Park ﬂoating
wind farm located at Buchan Deep 25 km east of Scotland, which is scheduled to become
operational by the end of 2017. In this section a brief description of the various types of
bottom-ﬁxed and ﬂoating foundations will be presented.
1.1.1
Bottom-Fixed Foundations
For completeness, the three most common bottom-ﬁxed foundation types; gravity based,
monopile and jacket, will be brieﬂy outlined in the following. On Figure 1.3 these three
types of support structures on varying water depths are shown.
Figure 1.3: Three common bottom-ﬁxed foundation types and their usual applicability. From left
to right: deep-water jacket, intermediate-water jacket, monopile and gravity based,
shallow-water monopile and gravity based [5].
==PAGE:28==
4
Chapter 1. Introduction
A gravity based support structure ensures the stability through a massive concrete struc-
ture located directly on the sea bed, on top of which the wind turbine is mounted.
Usually not used on depths beyond 25 m as the monopile here becomes more feasible. A
monopile is a hollow steel cylinder driven suﬃciently into the sea bed to ensure stability
of the wind turbine. Currently 88 % of installed oﬀshore wind capacity in Europe is
mounted on monopiles [6]. Monopiles are usually not used on water depths beyond 45
m. Going beyond 45 m water depths jacket structures are usually used, which are held in
place by pile foundations driven into the sea bed at each corner of the structure. They are
usually not considered feasible beyond 65 m water depths. Other types of bottom-ﬁxed
support structures also exists, such as e.g. tripods and suction buckets, but these will not
be discussed here.
1.1.2
Floating Foundations
Compared to bottom-ﬁxed foundations, a ﬂoating foundation (ﬂoater) is characterised
as a ﬂoating platform held in place by a mooring system, supporting a wind turbine
mounted on top. Generally, the ﬂoaters are grouped into three types with many variations,
modiﬁcations and combinations of these types proposed as alternative designs in recent
years, as discussed by e.g. [7]. On Figure 1.4 the three usual types of ﬂoaters, which are
actually well known concepts from the oil and gas industry, are shown. These are the
spar buoy (spar), semi-submersible (semisub) and tension leg platform (TLP) .
Figure 1.4: The three usual types of ﬂoating substructures. From left to right: spar buoy,
semi-submersible and tension leg platform [8].
A brief description of the three types of ﬂoaters are given in the following.
==PAGE:29==
1.1. Oﬀshore Wind Foundation Types
5
Spar Buoy
A spar is a long cylindrical platform which is ballast stabilised by virtue of a large
ballast mass located at the bottom of the relatively deep draught ﬂoater. Above the
ballast the platform is air-ﬁlled providing buoyancy. Consequently, the spar will
be inherently stable during e.g. towing as the centre of gravity is located below
the centre of buoyancy. However, due to its deep draught towing might not be a
possibility everywhere, hence assembly must occur oﬀshore. The spar is kept in
place by a catenary mooring system.
Semi-submersible
A semisub is a buoyancy and ballast stabilised (also having a large waterplane
area stiﬀness) relatively low draught ﬂoater. The structure is often seen with three
columns separated apart and connected by a latticed frame, with the wind turbine
mounted in either the centre of the ﬂoater or on one of the columns. Due to its
extensive and often high structural mass it is inherently stable as the spar, but con-
trary permits towing after e.g. onshore assembly because of its shallower draught,
which also entails wider applicability. Also moored with catenary moorings.
Tension Leg Platform
A TLP is a relatively compact and light ﬂoater which obtains its stability through the
mooring lines and buoyancy. The platform has a large excess amount of buoyancy
when submerged, which is counteracted by the vertical mooring lines, denoted
tethers. Because of this excess buoyancy the tethers are in constant tension and thus
consequently restraining especially vertical motions as well as rotations about the
horizontal axes. It is not self-stabilised and does as a result generally not permit
towing after its oﬀshore assembly. Susceptible to damage if a tether snaps.
The three above-mentioned types of ﬂoaters may be positioned within the so-called stabil-
ity triangle as visualised on Figure 1.5, with numerous other proposed concepts occupying
various locations within this triangle, depending on their stability characteristics.
Ballast stabilised
Mooring stabilised
Waterplane stabilised
Semisub
Spar
TLP
Figure 1.5: Floater type classiﬁcation in the stability triangle.
==PAGE:30==
6
Chapter 1. Introduction
Other types of ﬂoaters such as barges, multi-turbine ﬂoaters and hybrid wind/wave
platforms also exist, but will not be discussed in the present work. Regarding present full
scale ﬂoaters of the above-mentioned types, the Statoil Hywind spar and Principle Power
WindFloat semisub are currently in operation as demonstration projects. No full scale
TLP exists but the Glosten PelaStar is a proposed solution. The reader is referred to the
literature if more information on these ﬂoaters is desired.
As the spar, semisub and TLP naturally have diﬀerent characteristics, their respective
main advantages and disadvantages can be established for future reference. These may
be consulted in Table 1.1.
Table 1.1: Summary of main advantages and disadvantages of the three most common types of
proposed ﬂoating foundations, as discussed by [7] and [9].
Floater type
Advantages
Disadvantages
Spar
• Simple design
• Deep draught hinders
• Inherently stable
assembled towing ability

• Moderate dynamics

1.2
Motivation and Project Scope
Bringing down the cost of ﬂoating wind is essential for its future application and com-
mercial success. The above-mentioned ﬂoating platforms have for several decades been
utilised in the oﬀshore oil and gas industry, hence it could be argued that the technology
is proven and readily available. However, the mounting of a wind turbine on such a
ﬂoating platform signiﬁcantly alters the dynamics, since now not only waves but also
wind will signiﬁcantly aﬀect the coupled motions of the ﬂoater and the turbine. With
the commencement of the forthcoming 30 MW Hywind Scotland Pilot Park ﬂoating wind
farm later this year, consisting of ﬁve 6 MW turbines mounted on spars, Statoil hopes to
cut costs by two-thirds compared to its 2009 prototype [9](Hywind Demo) consisting of
a single 2.3 MW turbine also mounted on a spar. Just as was the case for bottom-ﬁxed
foundations a decade or two ago, acquired experience is immensely important and valu-
able as well for general cost-reductions. Nonetheless, as outlined in Table 1.1 spars have
==PAGE:31==
1.2. Motivation and Project Scope
7
several disadvantages still, i.e. their relative deep draught, heavy weight and resulting
requirement for oﬀshore assembly using heavy lift vessels with high day rates. [7] claims
that for a contemporary 7 MW wind turbine, the existing ﬂoaters are very heavy ranging
from 2,500 tonnes to 10,000 tonnes, and utilises construction methods acquired from the
shipbuilding and oil and gas industry, thus requiring months of fabrication at the port of
launch. [7] further argues that it is necessary to utilise the industrialised manufacturing
used in the onshore wind industry, to bring down the cost of ﬂoating wind remarkably.
Consequently, clever ﬂoater designs must be conceived, simulated and tested in near
future, while the mindset of designers must concurrently be changed from "we have
designed this structure, how do we manufacture it?" to "we must manufacture this way,
how do we design it?" [7].
While numerical models might not capture all eﬀects and phenomena compared to
physical model tests, they provide a relatively easy and inexpensive way to initially asses
the dynamic behaviour and responses of a proposed ﬂoater design, exposed to vari-
ous types of forcing, and are nonetheless thus a very important tool. In early design
stages where potentially many diﬀerent ﬂoater conﬁgurations need to be analysed, quick
frequency-domain numerical models are particularly useful due to their fast simulation
time, while still capturing the most important phenomena.
Albeit not producing as
detailed results as time-consuming time-domain numerical models. As a result, time-
domain simulations or even physical model tests may be conducted subsequently in order
to asses e.g. structural requirements and detailed design etc., once a proposed design has
been decided on using the quicker frequency-domain model.
Project Scope
The present work aims to suggest a site-dependant design of the TetraSpar type ﬂoater,
which is a new type of ﬂoater that tries to combine the best qualities of the spar, semisub
and TLP in a new, simple and lightweight structure, in the attempt to produce disruptive
costs within ﬂoating wind. The concept, developed by co-supervisor Henrik Stiesdal, in-
corporates a new and novel idea of a ballasted keel suspended in chains below the actual
ﬂoater to provide stability. The ﬂoater itself is to be constructed of components that can be
mass-produced away from the port of assembly, thus also emphasising industrialisation.
A frequency-domain numerical model of the USTUTT TripleSpar ﬂoater, which is a
publicly available concrete semi-submersible ﬂoater, that in this study supports the DTU
10 MW reference wind turbine, has been developed in Matlab at DTU Wind Energy,
and has additionally been tuned against a higher ﬁdelity time-domain numerical model.
Figures of the TripleSpar and TetraSpar concepts are shown on Figure 1.6. Initially, a
sensitivity analysis will be conducted on the TripleSpar ﬂoater in order to investigate
the eﬀects of varying a set of diﬀerent input parameters, and to generally explore the
ﬂoater design space and its various constraints and design limits. Considering two sig-
niﬁcantly diﬀerent TripleSpar conﬁgurations, the design space is subsequently explored
even further. Conducting appropriate model changes, the numerical model is altered to
==PAGE:32==
8
Chapter 1. Introduction
(a) TripleSpar render.
(b) TetraSpar render.
Figure 1.6: Sketches of the TripleSpar and TetraSpar concepts [10] [7].
consider the new TetraSpar type ﬂoater. By virtue of the ballast being suspended and
not directly a part of the ﬂoater, the eﬀects of varying ballast mass and ballast depth is
initially investigated. Next, two conﬁgurations of the concept are investigated, namely
a semisub variant and a spar variant, in order to establish their characteristics. Lastly, a
design of the TetraSpar type ﬂoater compliant with site conditions at Buchan Deep (lo-
cation for Hywind Scotland) will be proposed, by conducting a parametric investigation
on relevant ﬂoater parameters, derived from the TripleSpar sensitivity analysis. This
parametric investigation will be coupled to a cost function in order to determine the most
economically feasible design.
1.3
Related Works
Of relevant related works should be mentioned that of Tracy [11] who in 2007 did a
parametric design of a ﬂoating concrete ballasted steel cylinder supporting the NREL 5
MW baseline wind turbine. In that study the diameter and the draught (length of the
cylinder) were parametrised, while three diﬀerent mooring conﬁgurations were investi-
gated; TLP, slack catenary and taught catenery. By virtue of parametrising the diameter
and draught only, the design space was explored from ﬂoaters such as waterplane area
stabilised barges with a low draught to ballast stabilised ﬂoaters such as spar buoys hav-
ing a large draught, with the above-mentioned mooring conﬁgurations applied for each
ﬂoater. Although not coupled to a cost function in order to trade each design to one
another, the study did discuss the importance of minimising cost driving factors such as
==PAGE:33==
1.3. Related Works
9
the structure displacement and mooring line tension.
A more comprehensive and recent study is that of Hall [12] who in 2013 performed
an optimisation framework to enable a broad exploration of the design space of ﬂoat-
ing support structures for wind turbines, using appropriate simpliﬁcations. This study
initially involved replacing the geometric parametrisation with a hydrodynamics-based
approach. This was done in order to limit a priori assumptions about platform geom-
etry, but turned out to only capture the basic nature of the design space, and hence a
diﬀerent approach was subsequently employed. This approach was more conventional
using a frequency domain geometry-based optimisation routine involving nine variables,
in which several designs conﬁgurations consisting of TLPs, semi-submersibles and spars
were proposed, with varying dimensions and number of cylinders. Further, each design
was coupled to a cost function in order to evaluate the best performing structure relative
to cost. While this study resulted in a great amount of knowledge on design optimisation
of a wide variety of ﬂoating substructure conﬁgurations, it did not emphasise industri-
alisation, indicated by the fact that several of the best performing designs, are relative
complex and thus diﬃcult and expensive to manufacture on a vast industrialised scale.
Further, this study also utilised the NREL 5 MW baseline wind turbine. With industry’s
shift towards larger and larger wind turbines, a re-evaluation of Hall’s study with e.g.
the DTU 10 MW reference wind turbine, while concurrently imposing constraints on how
complex the designs are allowed to become, would be very interesting.
As part of the LIFES50+ project a very recent study [13] conducted in 2016 investigated
an optimisation framework in terms of cost and dynamic behaviour of a ﬂoating wind
turbine, with the aim of assisting designers in the conceptual design stage in ﬁnding the
most feasible solution more rapidly, in order to speed up the overall design process and
to minimise cost. The study focused on the USTUTT TripleSpar ﬂoater, and involved
four design parameters; column spacing, platform radius, heave plate height and ratio of
heave plate radius to platform radius. Further, the study included, within the numerical
model, a wind turbine controller in order to investigate the eﬀects of varying aerodynamic
damping on the system’s dynamic behaviour. While this study did indeed consider a
large turbine, which is expected to become the trend in the coming years, the utilised
ﬂoater is still a relatively large structure which most likely is diﬃcult to manufacture and
move due to its shear size, requiring large if not multiple cranes and a deep port to launch
it as well.
Other works concerning parametric design of ﬂoating substructures are also present
in the literature see e.g. [14], many of which with varying focus. In relation to the present
study the above-mentioned are of particular interest however.
The present study diﬀerentiates itself from the ﬁrst two mentioned works in the sense
that it employs a more contemporary 10 MW wind turbine instead of a 5 MW, which
is considered a medium-sized turbine by today’s standards (in June 2017 MHI Vestas
Oﬀshore Wind announced a 9.5 MW oﬀshore wind turbine, for additional details see
[15]).
Further, it will not consider all possible sorts of ﬂoater conﬁgurations ranging
==PAGE:34==
10
Chapter 1. Introduction
from TLPs to barges etc., but will emphasise a ﬂoater concept developed with exten-
sive industrialisation purposes in mind. In relation to the latter works, the same wind
turbine is considered, but the USTUTT TripleSpar ﬂoater, while shown to be a viable
concept, is a massive structure thus diﬃcult to manufacture on an industrialised scale.
The present work will initially conduct a sensitivity analysis on the TripleSpar, in order
to establish knowledge of various input-output relations and general dynamic charac-
teristics for future reference, since a tuned numerical model of this ﬂoater is available,
and that a simpliﬁed representation of the TetraSpar ﬂoater can be made to resemble the
TripleSpar ﬂoater. Further, no blade pitch-to-feather controller will be included in the
present numerical model.
1.4
Thesis Structure
The remainder of the thesis is organised into the following chapters.
Chapter 2
In this chapter a brief overview and description of relevant theory and assump-
tions of the QuLAF numerical model and WAMIT software are given. Further, a
description of the TripleSpar ﬂoater is also included.
Chapter 3
In this chapter the results from the sensitivity analysis on the TripleSpar ﬂoater
are presented. In addition, the constraints related to ﬂoater design are investigated.
Lastly, the design space is further explored by considering two alternative TripleSpar
conﬁgurations.
Chapter 4
In this chapter the numerical model is adapted to consider the TetraSpar type ﬂoater.
Initially, investigations on the suspended ballast characteristics and diﬀerent ﬂoater
conﬁgurations are conducted. Subsequently, the model is coupled to a cost function
and a Buchan Deep compliant design is proposed.
Chapter 5
In this chapter the concluding comments of the study as well as recommendations
and suggestions to future work is provided.

Chapter2
Theory and Setup of Numerical Model
In this chapter the main theoretical background of the applied numerical model is out-
lined. A brief description of the WAMIT simulations is provided. Further, brief descrip-
tions of the DTU 10 MW reference wind turbine and the TripleSpar ﬂoater are provided.
For more detailed descriptions of the theory used, the reader is referred to the literature.
2.1
Description of the QuLAF Numerical Model
QuLAF is the name of a numerical model developed in Matlab. It was originally devel-
oped as a model for Quick Load Analysis, QuLA, of bottom-ﬁxed oﬀshore wind turbines,
at the Department of Wind Energy at DTU [16]. The QuLA model has recently been
extended by PhD candidate at DTU Wind Energy, Antonio M. P. Jurado, to a ﬂoating
foundation as well, hence the extension Floating. The model as well as various additions
and modiﬁcations imposed by this work’s author, will be brieﬂy outlined in this section.
This model serves as the basis for the numerical results computed in this study.
Generally, in the early design stages of e.g. ﬂoating foundations, time-domain nu-
merical models give very detailed results, although they are very computationally heavy,
when analysing ﬂoater responses under various environmental conditions. This is espe-
cially the case if several diﬀerent ﬂoater conﬁgurations and designs are to be evaluated.
Hence, in the initial design stages, it is beneﬁcial to utilize a quick model, which allows for
the analysis of many diﬀerent designs, before investigating the chosen design in a more
comprehensive state-of-the-art time-domain model. QuLAF is a quick linear frequency-
domain model allowing eﬃcient computation of various ﬂoater designs. It takes input
from WAMIT to account for diﬀraction and radiation, while viscous eﬀects are modelled
through the Morison drag term. Further, aerodynamic loads may be either generated
internally by QuLAF or precomputed loads and aerodynamic damping from FAST may
be applied. Lastly, in this study the mooring system will be linearised around the ﬂoater’s
equilibrium position.
11
==PAGE:36==
12
Chapter 2. Theory and Setup of Numerical Model
2.1.1
Time-domain and Frequency-domain Conversion
QuLAF is a frequency-domain model which allows for fast simulation times, which is
often necessary in conceptual design stages. It is however possible to shift back and
forth between the frequency- and time-domain by virtue of a Fourier transform.
To
do this an FFT algorithm is used which computes the DFT sequence or its inverse IFFT,
depending from which domain transformation occurs. Brieﬂy, the FFT algorithm reduces
the complexity of computing the DFT from O(n2) to O(nlogn) where n is the data size
[17]. For a discrete time, t, or angular frequency, ω, the DFT, which is applied when going
from the time-domain to the frequency-domain, is given as
X(k) =
N−1
X
n=0
x(n)e−i2πkn/N,
for
k = 0,1,2,...,N −1
(2.1)
in which N is the number of data points. Conversely, a signal in the frequency-domain
can be transformed to the time-domain through the inverse transformation given by
x(n) = 1
N
N−1
X
k=0
X(k)ei2πkn/N,
for
n = 0,1,2,...,N −1
(2.2)
Such transformations are useful when investigating the responses of a ﬂoater, while still
retaining computational eﬃciency and speed.
2.1.2
Equations of Motion
Within oﬀshore engineering and naval architecture the six degrees of freedom (DoF) are
denoted as follows. Translation in x; surge. Rotation about x; roll. Translation in y;
sway. Rotation about y; pitch. Translation in z; heave. Rotation about z; yaw. On Figure
2.1.2 these deﬁnitions are visualised along with the wind and wave direction used in this
study, which is coincident with surge.

y
Heave
Sway
Surge
Roll
Pitch
Yaw
Wind & wave direction
Figure 2.1: Coordinate system with deﬁnitions.
QuLAF does however not model all six DoFs, but only what is considered the most
signiﬁcant in early design stages; namely surge, heave and pitch of the ﬂoater, as well as
==PAGE:37==
2.1. Description of the QuLAF Numerical Model
13
tower modal deﬂection as the fourth. QuLAF is thus a 3+1 DoF model.
The responses of the ﬂoater are computed using the equations of motion (EoM) in the
frequency domain which read

−ω2(M + A(ω)) + iωB(ω) + C

ξ(ω) = F(ω)
(2.3)
in which ω is the angular frequency, M is the mass matrix, A(ω) is the frequency dependent
added mass matrix, B(ω) is the frequency dependent damping matrix, C is the stiﬀness
matrix, ξ(ω) is the response vector and F(ω) is the matrix of complex forces, i.e. sum of
wind and wave forcing including WAMIT wave excitation and Morison drag, given as
F(ω) = Fhydro(ω) + Faero(ω)
(2.4)
= ˆηX(ω) + Fdrag(ω) + Faero(ω)
(2.5)
in which ˆη is the complex surface elevation, X(ω) is the complex wave excitation force
from WAMIT per unit wave amplitude, Fdrag(ω) is the complex Morison viscous drag
forcing and Faero(ω) is the complex aerodynamic forcing.
Rewriting the EoM the response can be computed as
ξ(ω) =
F(ω)
−ω2(M + A(ω)) + iωB(ω) + C
(2.6)
In Appendix E a description of the RAO entity is further provided.
In order to model the wind turbine tower, only the ﬁrst mode shape is included. While
the three ﬁrst DoFs are surge, heave and pitch, the fourth DoF is the tower top fore-aft
deﬂection, δ(t), which is computed as
δ(t) = α(t)φhub
(2.7)
in which α(t) is the general coordinate of the tower top deﬂection and φhub is the mode
shape at hub height which is known a priori. Knowing the tower top deﬂection, the
nacelle acceleration, anac, can be computed in the time-domain through
anac(t) = ¨x1(t) + ¨x3hhub(t) + ¨α(t)φhub
(2.8)
in which x1(t) is the surge response and x3(t) is the pitch response in the time-domain and
where a dot denotes diﬀerentiation with respect to time. hhub is the turbine hub height.
The natural frequencies of the system, which are the surge, heave, pitch and tower
natural frequencies, are computed by solving the generalised eigenvalue problem [18]
using the built-in Matlab function eig

C −ω2


vi = 0
(2.9)
where vi are the eigenvectors.

2.1.4
Linear Monochromatic Wave Kinematics
Linear (Airy) wave theory imposes several assumptions on the description of the propa-
gating waves and ﬂuid ﬂow. These are the assumptions that the wave height relative to
the water depth is small on shallow water (small amplitudes), and that the wave height
relative to the wave length is small on deep water (small steepness). Further assumptions
such that the ﬂuid ﬂow must be ideal [19], and that the ﬂuid ﬂow must be planar and
propagating on a constant ﬂat bottom are also imposed [17]. Linear waves oﬀer for most
cases suﬃcient accuracy when investigating wave-structure interaction. If needed, non-
linear eﬀects can be estimated from the ﬁrst order (linear) results.
Monochromatic waves propagate with a single frequency and has thus only one pe-
riod. Monochromatic waves are consequently harmonic in time and space, and the free
surface elevation, η, propagating in the positive x-direction, can therefore be described
sinusoidally by e.g. a cosine function through
η(x,t) = H
2 cos(ωt −kx)
(2.29)
in which H is the wave height, ω is the angular frequency of the wave period, t is the
time, x is the horizontal coordinate and k is the wave number. On Figure 2.2 a description
sketch showing a monochromatic wave with parameters in 2D is shown.
z
η

reproduced from [20].

ω = 2π

where T is the wave period and λ is the wave length.
The wave period, and thus wave frequency, is related to the wave number or wave
length through the linear dispersion relation which reads
ω2 = gk tanhkh
(2.32)
where h is the water depth.
Further assuming that the ﬂow is incompressible and irrotational, allows for the
application of a potential ﬂow formulation, which entails that the velocity ﬁeld can be
described by the gradient of a scalar function; the velocity potential [21].
The time-domain velocity potential for regular waves is given as [20]
φ(x,z,t) = −ωH

2.1.5
Linear Irregular Wave Kinematics
A sea state is the term covering a wave climate at a given location, for a certain amount of
time e.g. six hours [20]. An irregular or random sea state is composed of an (in principle)
inﬁnite number of superpositioned monochromatic waves, which may be added on top
of each other owing to linearisation [20]. In simulations the sea state is on the other hand
composed of a very large number of monochromatic waves, not inﬁnite. Each of these
monochromatic waves may have diﬀerent periods, phases and amplitudes.
Irregular sea states are characterised by the so-called signiﬁcant wave height, Hs, and
peak wave period, Tp. The signiﬁcant wave height is a measure of the mean wave height,
of the 1/3 highest waves in a given wave record, while the peak period is the period
at which the maximum amount of energy in the sea state is located at. To deal with
irregular waves in a practical sense, a wave spectrum is introduced. The wave spectrum
is a statistical approach used to determine how the energy in a given sea state will be
distributed amongst the wave frequencies.
The QuLAF model employs the JONSWAP (Joint North Sea Wave Project) spectrum,
==PAGE:42==

and lastly the power a is given as

As both regular and irregular wave kinematics are computed using linear wave theory,
they are only computed up to SWL. In order to evaluate the kinematics at the actual
surface elevation, e.g. at the crest, stretching of the kinematics must be applied. This can
be done e.g. by virtue of Wheeler stretching, where the velocities from the sea bed to SWL
are stretched and consequently applied between the sea bed and the surface elevation,
which is linearly consistent since the shift is only proportional to η [17]. In the present
simulations no stretching of the wave kinematics are applied.
2.1.6
Linear Focused Waves
The surface elevation for a focused wave is in the present study also computed using
a JONSWAP spectrum. The diﬀerence between irregular waves and a focused wave is
that for a focused wave, all wave components share a mutual focus point, consequently
resulting in a dramatic surface elevation at this speciﬁc point. In order to achieve this focus
point denoted by (x0,t0), no phase diﬀerences between the individual wave components
are present, and the surface elevation is thus given by
η(x,t) =
N
X
i=1
ai cos(ωi(t −t0) −k1(x −x0))
(2.48)
The actual wave kinematics are computed according to the description given for the
irregular waves. Supervisor Henrik Bredmose kindly provided a script for generating
focused waves.
Despite the very non-linear characteristics of a focused wave, a linear description is
used here in consistency with the linear numerical model.
==PAGE:44==
20
Chapter 2. Theory and Setup of Numerical Model
2.1.7
Hydrodynamic Forcing
The hydrodynamic viscous eﬀects are modelled through the Morison drag force (drag
term) [20]. In the x-direction a strip theory approach is used, where the ﬂoater is discre-
tised in the vertical direction. The resulting drag force is then found by integrating these
individual force components. In the vertical direction only the wave kinematics at the
bottom of the ﬂoater must be taken into account. The forcing reads
Fdrag,x =
Z ztop
zbot
1
2ρwCDxD(u −˙x1)|u −˙x1| dz
(2.49)
Fdrag,z = 1
2ρwCDzD(w −˙x2)|w −˙x2|
(2.50)
where ρw is the water density, which in the present study is taken as 1025 kg/m3, CDx
is the surge drag coeﬃcient, CDz is the heave drag coeﬃcient and D is the diameter of
the ﬂoater column. Note that in the equations above the relative velocity of the ﬂoater is
shown. In order to maintain computational eﬃciency in this study, the relative velocities
will consequently not be taken into account, thus only the wave kinematics are considered.
The total hydrodynamic forcing is lastly found by summing the contributions from each
ﬂoater column, in each DoF.
2.1.8
Aerodynamic Forcing
The aerodynamic loads acting on the turbine, in the following denoted thrust, are re-
sponsible for generating the torque which is what in brief generates electricity via the
generator located within the nacelle. Correct computation of the thrust is thus vital in
order to determine the amount of power produced. But it is also of severe importance to
the dynamics of the turbine, especially on ﬂoating substructures which inevitably will be
more lively than their ﬁxed counterparts. Initially, it is necessary to obtain a wind ﬁeld,
which may be either measured or modelled. QuLAF employs two methods for obtaining
the wind ﬁeld; either compute the wind ﬁeld internally within QuLAF in a relatively
simple manner, or utilizing data obtained from a higher ﬁdelity model, in this case FAST.
The ﬁrst method entails computing a wind spectrum based on a Kaimal wind spectrum,
Swind, which reads [17]
Swind(f ) =
4I2V10l

1 + 6 f l
V10
5/3
(2.51)
in which I is the turbulence intensity, f is the frequency, V10 is the 10-min averaged wind
speed and l is the turbulence length scale which is expressed in terms of the following
conditional expression
l =

5.67z,
if z < 60 m
340.2 m,
if z > 60 m
(2.52)
in which z is the vertical location.
After obtaining the Kaimal spectral density function from a desired 10-min wind
==PAGE:45==
2.1. Description of the QuLAF Numerical Model
21
speed, the actual and ﬂuctuating time series of the wind speed can be computed through
V (t) = V10 + Vb(t)
(2.53)
where Vb(t) is the ﬂuctuating wind speed component, which in turn is given as
Vb(t) =
N
X
i=1
bi cos(ωit + ϕi)
(2.54)
where ωi is the angular frequency and ϕi is a random number in the interval 0 to 2π. The
amplitudes bi are computed from the Kaimal spectrum through
bi =
p
2Swind(f )∆f
(2.55)
in which ∆f is the employed frequency resolution.
Once the wind ﬁeld has been determined, the actual aerodynamic loads or thrust,
Fwind, can be computed by virtue of
Fwind = 1
2ρairArotorCT Vrel|Vrel|
(2.56)
in which ρair is the density of air, Arotor is the swept area of the blades, CT is the thrust
coeﬃcient and Vrel is the relative wind speed at hub height given as
Vrel = Vhub −˙xhub
(2.57)
where xhub is the hub displacement and a dot denotes diﬀerentiation w.r.t. time. Note
again that relative velocities are not applied in the present study. On Figure 2.3 the thrust
coeﬃcient and computed thrust as functions of wind speed may be observed. The thrust
coeﬃcients are from Table 3.5 in the DTU 10 MW RWT deﬁnition report [22], and the
thrust is computed based on eq. 2.56. Values for CT are only listed for integer values in
the interval 4-25 m/s, i.e. between cut-in and cut-out. Knowing that the thrust at rated
wind speed of 11.4 m/s should obviously be largest, the value of CT from 8-11 m/s is
extended to 11.4 m/s as well in order to produce a correct visualisation. Further note that
after rated wind speed is reached, the blades pitch and thus feather out of the wind in
order to reduce the thrust force, the generated power however is the same from rated to
cut-oﬀ.

The second method entails employing the FAST precomputed loads which come shipped
with QuLAF. These are arguable more accurate than using eq. 2.56, which in its form is a
relative simple expression for the computation of the thrust force. In the present work the
FAST precomputed loads have been utilised when modelling ﬂuctuating wind in order
to obtain the most reliable results. When running the model with static wind forcing, i.e.
constant wind speed, eq. 2.56 has been utilised due to the FAST loads being ﬂuctuating
in time and not static. Upscaling of the FAST precomputed loads follows the procedure
outlined in Appendix C. Further, aerodynamic damping is also precomputed in FAST,
and is included for ﬂuctuating wind simulations.
2.1.9
Floater Inertia Calculations
As many diﬀerent ﬂoater conﬁgurations are to be investigated, the mass moment of inertia
of the ﬂoater will inevitably change. Hence, for each diﬀerent geometry, the inertia of the
ﬂoater is computed in a Matlab function. The calculations may be consulted in Appendix
A.
2.1.10
Linearised Catenary Mooring System
The purpose of the mooring system is to keep the structure against moving too drastically
from combined wind, current and wave loading. The mooring system utilized here for the
TripleSpar consists of a spread mooring system involving three catenary cables attached
120 deg to each other as outlined in [23], with two cables upwind and one downwind. It
has been ensured that a signiﬁcant amount of the cables are resting on the sea bed, such
that no vertical forces are present in the anchor points, which are assumed as gravity
anchors. The mooring conﬁguration is sketched on Figure 2.4
==PAGE:47==

2.2
WAMIT Computations
WAMIT® is a radiation/diﬀraction program for the linear analysis of surface wave inter-
action with oﬀshore structures, originally developed at MIT. For a detailed description
on the theoretical background of WAMIT, the reader is referred to the comprehensive
==PAGE:52==
28
Chapter 2. Theory and Setup of Numerical Model
descriptions given in the WAMIT Version 7 User Manual[25], which may be downloaded
freely from the internet. Further, [19] also provides plentiful descriptions of relevant
potential ﬂow theory and its assumptions.
The program allows for the analysis of either one or multiple interacting bodies in
ﬁnite or inﬁnite water depths. These bodies may be located anywhere in between the free
surface and the sea bed. In addition, the bodies may be deﬁned as freely ﬂoating, ﬁxed in
position for a given or all DoFs and lastly also restrained in motion by e.g. mooring lines.
The program employs a potential formulation of the 3D panel method approach, based
on the distribution of sources and normal dipoles.
In the present works WAMIT has solely been utilized to evaluate the hydrodynamic
database; i.e. the hydrostatic stiﬀness matrix, Chydro, frequency dependent hydrody-
namic added mass matrix, A(ω), frequency dependent potential/radiation damping ma-
trix, Bpotential(ω) as well as the frequency dependent complex amplitude vector of the
exciting forces, X(ω). Here follows a brief description of the WAMIT simulation setup.
2.2.1
Computational Setup and Execution
WAMIT consists of two fundamental subprograms, POTEN and FORCE, which solves
for diﬀerent parameters and are run sequentially. POTEN solves for the radiation and
diﬀraction velocity potentials on the body surface. FORCE computes, amongst other,
the hydrodynamic parameters outlined in the beginning of this section, i.e. hydrostatic
stiﬀness, hydrodynamic added mass, radiation damping and exciting forces.
WAMIT does not have a graphical user interface but instead relies on it being executed
through commands speciﬁed via a DOS Command Prompt Window. Consequently, all
the necessary information for the program must be submitted via a set of input ﬁles
brieﬂy outlined in the following.
Geometric Data File (.gdf)
In the WAMIT computations the body geometry is deﬁned by a number of connected
four-sided panels (low-order method). In this sense, the Geometric Data File holds
the coordinates of these panel vertices of the discretised surface only. Supervisor
Michael Borg kindly provided a Matlab script for the generation of these panel
vertices, with a possibility to reﬁne the mesh of the discretised surface. This script
was later extended by the author to include more columns for applicability with
the TetraSpar ﬂoater. Due to the geometrical setup of the ﬂoater, a symmetry-plane
located at y = 0 has been utilized in order to cut down on computational eﬀort. In
relation to the reﬁnement of the mesh, DNV has published a recommended practice;
DNV-RP-C205 [26]. Section 7.3.4 on frequency and panel mesh requirements, states
that for a low-order panel method the diagonal length of one panel should be
less than 1/6 of the smallest wave length analysed. A convergence analysis on the
number of panels required to fulﬁl this criteria has been conducted, and may be
consulted in Appendix B.
==PAGE:53==
2.3. DTU 10MW Reference Wind Turbine
29
Potential Control File (.pot)
Input to the POTEN subprogram is submitted via the Potential Control File. In this
ﬁle the water depth, wave periods and incident wave heading angles are deﬁned.
In the present computations the water depth was set to h = 180 m as described in
[23], wave periods T were selected in the range 1.5 s to 625 s and wave heading
angles β were set to -180 deg to 180 deg in increments of 10 deg, yielding 37 heading
angles in total. This was done in order to make sure that all nonzero damping and
signiﬁcant wave forcing was evaluated.
Force Control File (.frc)
Input to the FORCE subprogram is submitted via the Force Control File. In this ﬁle
the water density, coordinates of the body CoG as well as the speciﬁcation of which
hydrodynamic parameters to evaluate and output are deﬁned. Further, a possibility
to include an external mass matrix, damping matrix or stiﬀness matrix is present.
As mentioned earlier, WAMIT is used only to compute hydrodynamic parameters,
and not the responses as this is done internally in QuLAF, hence no external mass,
damping or stiﬀness matrix is included. Moreover, the total mass of the ﬂoater is
determined when conducting the inertia calculations, and as a results of this, the
draught is also computed. The draught is needed when creating the mesh in the
Geometric Data File.
Conﬁguration File (.cfg)
A Conﬁguration File permits speciﬁcation of various simulation options and set-
tings. For the present simulations some of the chosen options are e.g. the option
to apply the low-order panel method as outlined above, and the option to solve for
the scattering problem. In order to correctly implement the zero-thickness dipole
panels on the column heave plates, the latter option has to be included in the simu-
lations. On dipole panels the potential is not based on the source formulation, but
instead represented by dipoles alone. When conducting the WAMIT simulations
the linear system of equations can be solved by several diﬀerent methods, where two
of these has been utilized in this project. Normally WAMIT solves the equations by
utilizing a special iterative solver employing a maximum value of iterations (default
35), if convergence is not achieved the computations continue without interruption
but issues a warning message. When conducting the convergence analysis, it was
necessary to utilize the direct solver instead, as the iterative solver did not converge,
or atleast required an inconvenient amount of iteration steps to achieve convergence.
2.3
DTU 10MW Reference Wind Turbine
The wind turbine used in the present study is the DTU 10MW reference wind turbine,
developed by researchers at DTU Wind Energy. The turbine is not commercially available,
since it is designed solely with the academic purpose of application in numerical models
==PAGE:54==
30
Chapter 2. Theory and Setup of Numerical Model
such as the present study, and to benchmark other future designs. Note that no full size
model of this turbine exists. The wind turbine is characterised by the parameters listed
in Table 2.2.
In the present study a tower shortened by 25 m has been applied, due to the geometry
of the TripleSpar ﬂoater’s tripod transition piece as discussed in [23]. This means that the
tower base diameter was changed from 8.3 m to 7.7 m, the length of the tower shortened
from 115.63 m to 90.63 m and the tower mass reduced from 628,442 kg to 432,955 kg.
Elastic properties was adopted from [22] and the hub height was maintained. Further
note that in relation to shortening the tower, the tower bending natural frequency was
increased from 0.25 Hz to 0.4 Hz.

2.4
Baseline TripleSpar Floater
QuLAF is originally developed for the scaled down version of the USTUTT TripleSpar.
The TripleSpar ﬂoater developed by the University of Stuttgart is a publicly available
concrete semi-submersible ﬂoater. It consists of three concrete columns with heave plates
mounted at the bottom. The columns are connected by a tripod structure inspired from
the BARD Tripile. On top of the tripod the shortened version of the DTU 10MW turbine
==PAGE:55==

pitch. Here a discrepancy between [23] and Table 2.3 of 18 % larger inertia in pitch for the
present computations. Despite extensive troubleshooting the cause for this discrepancy
has not been found.
The discrepancy however will be the same for all subsequently
investigated designs, hence it is not directly critical as parameter results may still be
compared mutually. The TripleSpar conﬁguration listed in Table 2.3 will throughout
this report be referred to as the baseline TripleSpar. On Figure 2.6 a sketch showing the

Chapter3
TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis and
Parameter Constraints
In this chapter results from a parametric sensitivity analysis of the TripleSpar ﬂoater
when varying various ﬂoater parameters are presented and analysed. The aim of this
sensitivity analysis is to explore the design space and to establish which ﬂoater parameters
are important when investigating and modifying certain ﬂoater properties; in this case
surge, heave and pitch response, nacelle fore-aft acceleration and the natural frequencies.
During this sensitivity analysis the following parameters related to the ﬂoater have
been chosen to be investigated
• ballast mass
• column diameter
• column spacing
• column length
• mooring line length
• mooring line mass
These parameters are further visualised on Figure 3.1 for clariﬁcation. Once the sensitivity
analysis has been conducted, it is additionally possible to examine the inﬂuence of these
parameters on the chosen set of ﬂoater constraints for e.g. the nacelle fore-aft acceleration
and natural frequency ranges.
Lastly, with this augmented knowledge of the design
space, two alternative TripleSpar conﬁgurations may be designed to comply with the
chosen constraints.
33

3.1
TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis
Conducting a parametric sensitivity analysis on the numerical model of the TripleSpar
ﬂoater, should entail augmented insight into how the values of the input parameters aﬀect
the output of the model i.e. responses and natural frequencies. This can subsequently be
utilised to justify design-wise decisions when designing a ﬂoating substructure resem-
bling, in this case, the TripleSpar concept. Additionally, the input-output connection may
for some parameters be found to be negligible or even non-existent, hence the analysis
also aims to clarify redundant system connections and parameters, which consequently
should not be considered in full detail when designing alternative substructure conﬁgu-
rations later on. Lastly, by virtue of a sensitivity analysis unexpected connections, which
may not be envisaged directly, can also be clariﬁed.
The present sensitivity analysis is of the one-variable-at-a-time method, and is thus
relatively simplistic. The method involves varying one of the above-outlined input pa-
rameters at a time in a pre-determined interval, while keeping the remaining parameters
constant at their baseline values, where the baseline values are those for the baseline
TripleSpar ﬂoater as listed in Table 2.3. Once the investigated input parameter has been
varied in the desired interval, it is returned to its own baseline value, and the next input
parameter is varied in a similar manner. While this approach is very good to establish a
simple relationship between input and output, it does however not take mutual interac-
tion between input parameters into account i.e. when varying multiple input parameters
simultaneously. Despite this limitation it still provides an adequate relationship between
input-output connections needed for the present purposes. When varying the input pa-
rameters other ﬂoater properties such as e.g. total ﬂoater mass and thus also draught
==PAGE:59==
3.1. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis
35
are slaved. This means that for instance, increasing the ballast mass also increases the
total mass of the ﬂoater and thus also the draught. Further, due to increased draught the
height of the fairleads decrease resulting in lower mooring restoring stiﬀness. As a con-
sequence of these slaved relations WAMIT computations have been carried out for each
new geometry, that is, for each input parameter value in its interval. For simplicity the
mass contribution from the wind turbine has been neglected when computing the ﬂoater
draught, since the total mass of the wind turbine at 1107 tonnes only constitutes 3.66 %
of the mass of the baseline ﬂoater without the wind turbine. However, the contribution
from the wind turbine to the general system matrices etc. has indeed been included as
explained in Chapter 2.
The sensitivity analysis has been carried out for three diﬀerent types of forcing; static
wind forcing, regular wave forcing and focused wave forcing, with only a single type of
forcing present at each time. The wind speed is held constant at the wind turbines rated
wind speed, however the corresponding thrust has been multiplied by a factor of two in
order to simulate conditions where extreme gusts of wind may occur. Relevant param-
eters for the three types of applied forcing in the sensitivity analysis may be consulted
in Table 3.1, with the forcing further visualised on Figure 3.2. A six hour sea state was
applied as recommended by [26]. Note that when determining the resulting responses in
the sensitivity analysis, the maximum absolute value has been considered for each DoF,
since initial possible transients have been ﬁltered. All results are computed on a 180 m
water depth.
Table 3.1: Overview of relevant parameters related to the applied forcing in the sensitivity
analysis.

The ﬁrst four sub-ﬁgures show the response in surge, heave and pitch as well as the nacelle
fore-aft acceleration, respectively, all for the three diﬀerent types of forcing considered.
In addition to this, on the same sub-ﬁgures, the surge, heave, pitch and tower natural
frequencies are also plotted. The motion response is read oﬀon the left y-axis while the
natural frequency is read oﬀon the right y-axis. In addition to these four sub-ﬁgures
are two sub-ﬁgures which may be consulted to explain and comprehend the obtained
results. The ﬁrst one of these contains the mooring stiﬀness and a measure of the applied
hydrodynamic forcing (explained below), in both surge, heave and pitch (subscript 11, 22

A measure of the hydrodynamic forcing acting on the ﬂoater, σF, relative to the
amount of energy in the sea state, ση, denoted fhyd, (can be interpreted as a force RAO) is
computed through
fhyd = σF

(3.1)
in which X is the sum of the complex wave excitation forces and complex viscous drag
forces, while Sη is the spectral values of the sea state, presently the focused wave. In
addition to this, for all of the six input parameters investigated, plots of the WAMIT
hydrodynamic properties and computed RAOs have been included in Appendix E for the
two outermost investigated input parameter values. This appendix will be referred to in
detail under each following subsection.
The numerical model of the baseline TripleSpar ﬂoater has been tuned against FAST
as previously mentioned, see Appendix C for details. Conducting the sensitivity anal-
ysis and varying the set of input parameters will naturally modify the ﬂoater response
characteristics. It was decided, due to the availability of this tuned model where the drag
coeﬃcients are well-calibrated, to tune all subsequent geometries against the damping
==PAGE:61==
3.1. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis
37
ratios of the baseline TripleSpar ﬂoater, thus using the same procedure as in [13]. The
reasoning for doing this is that despite the varying of the input parameters, the overall ge-
ometric appearance will still resemble the baseline TripleSpar (still having three columns
etc.), and hence the damping ratios can be assumed to be constant for all geometries. The
damping ratios for the TripleSpar are determined in Appendix D using QuLAF. While
[13] also lists a set of damping ratios for the TripleSpar, it was decided to re-compute these
using QuLAF, as it is not stated how they were determined and which model, including
assumptions, was used. The applied tuning of stiﬀness was left as it was.
The tuned damping contribution to the total system damping is determined the fol-
lowing way. For convenience, consider the homogeneous EoM in the time-domain which
reads
(M + A(ω)) ¨x + B(ω) ˙x + Cx = 0
(3.2)
where x is the response and a dot denotes diﬀerentiation with respect to time. Dividing
the expression with (M + A(ω)) yields
¨x +

(3.3)

given as [18]

where for the frequency-dependent matrices, the value at the natural frequency has been
used. Alternatively, one could also use the value for ω →∞. Note that despite the matrix
==PAGE:62==
38
Chapter 3. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis and Parameter Constraints
notation used here for consistency, damping ratios are only computed in surge, heave and
pitch thus only the three ﬁrst diagonal elements of the matrices are relevant in this regard
(actually three scalars), and the method consequently neglects cross-couplings. Once
determined for each computation, Badd has been added to the total system damping
matrix B(ω) as expressed by eq. (3.8), when computing the results.
In the following subsections results from the sensitivity analysis are presented.
3.1.1
Ballast Mass
The sensitivity analysis on the ballast mass, which is located at the bottom of each of the
three columns, has been conducted by varying the total amount of ballast shared by these
columns in the interval -20 % and +20 % of the amount located in the baseline TripleSpar,
in increments of 4 %. Note that while the total ballast mass is varied, the amount of ballast
mass will still be distributed equally between all three columns. Varying the amount of
ballast mass entails no modiﬁcations to the external geometry of the ﬂoater. However,
as the ballast mass naturally aﬀects the total mass of the ﬂoater, the draught changes
with each increment. Further, modifying the ballast mass will also entail changes to the
amount of physical space the ballast occupies within the ﬂoater columns, i.e. how high
the ballast protrudes from the bottom.
On Figure 3.3 the results from the ballast mass sensitivity analysis may be seen. In
Appendix E.1 the WAMIT hydrodynamic properties and RAOs for the two outermost
values in the investigated ballast mass interval may be consulted.
Surge motion
It is observed that when increasing the amount of ballast, the surge response due to the
static wind forcing is increasing. The cause for this is that when increasing the amount
of ballast, the total mass of the ﬂoater is also increased, hence a decrease in freeboard
inevitably occurs. A lowering of the freeboard entails that the surge mooring stiﬀness
decreases, because the fairleads are located at ﬁxed positions on the ﬂoater columns and
thus move up and down simultaneously with the freeboard.
The response to regular waves and a focused wave look similar and increase slightly
with increased ballast mass, albeit with diﬀerent magnitudes due to the focused wave
being most severe. Again, some increase is expected because of the lower surge mooring
stiﬀness. Additionally, when the ﬂoater draught is increased, the wave forcing on the
ﬂoater is also seen to increase, simply due to the fact that the area seen by the wave
particles is now larger, since the ﬂoater extends deeper down into the water.
Regarding the surge natural frequency, due to the increased mass and resulting larger
draught, the hydrodynamic added mass will also increase.
This combined with the
decreasing surge mooring stiﬀness results in that the surge natural frequency is decreasing
with increasing ballast mass. Note that the increase in added mass is not suﬃcient to
==PAGE:63==
3.1. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis
39
counteract the lower restoring stiﬀness and increased wave forcing, as the wave responses
tend to increase.
Heave motion
In heave the response caused by the static wind forcing will be zero due to the linear basis
of the numerical model (no or negligible vertical wind forces).
The heave responses due to wave forcing on the other hand are seen to decrease with
increasing ballast mass. The heave mooring stiﬀness will decrease due to the increasing
draught as discussed. It is however also observed that the heave hydrodynamic forcing
is decreasing with increasing ballast mass. As the draught is increased and the ﬂoater
thus sinks lower into the water, the vertical particle velocities acting at the bottom of the
ﬂoater gets smaller, hence also the vertical hydrodynamic force decreases. Lastly, the
heave RAO peak shifts further away from the wave forcing frequency. Despite the lower
heave mooring stiﬀness, the resulting increase in ﬂoater mass and lower hydrodynamic
forcing coupled with the RAO peak shift are more pronounced, and consequently results
in lower responses.
The heave natural frequency is decreasing with increasing ballast mass. Again as for
surge, the heave mooring stiﬀness decreases and the total ﬂoater mass increases, entailing
a lowering of the natural frequency.
Pitch motion
In pitch the response due to the static wind forcing is decreasing with increasing ballast
mass. It is initially noted that the pitch response is decreasing despite the pitch mooring
stiﬀness also decreasing, again due to increased draught. However, because the moment
arm between the buoyancy force and gravity force increases, the hydrostatic stiﬀness
increases. This will entail a lowering of the pitch response. Additionally, as the ﬂoater
freeboard decreases with increasing ballast mass, the hub height also decreases thus
lowering the torque arm when computing the pitch forcing (a torque). These factors
combined are more pronounced than the lowering of the pitch stiﬀness, and consequently
yields a lowering of the static wind pitch response.
The pitch response due to wave forcing is on the other hand increasing with increasing
ballast mass. The pitch mooring stiﬀness is as previously mentioned decreasing with
increasing ballast, hence some increase in response is suspected owing to this eﬀect. In
addition, it is noted that the hydrodynamic forcing in pitch is increasing. As the draught
gets larger, the surge forcing will increase as the submerged area seen by the horizontal
particles gets larger. The opposite holds true for the heave forcing, where the vertical
particle velocities decrease. Pitch hydrodynamic forcing is a torque stemming from the
surge and heave hydrodynamic forcing, plus the WAMIT excitation force contribution
which in pitch is observed to increase. Further, pitch ﬂoater inertia, added mass and
damping (and hydrostatic stiﬀness) are also increasing with increased ballast mass, hence
==PAGE:64==
40
Chapter 3. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis and Parameter Constraints
the pitch motion can be hard to predict. Investigating the pitch RAO the peak shifts
towards higher frequencies as additional ballast mass is added, and thus closer to the
wave forcing frequencies, which can consequently explain the increasing pitch response.
The pitch natural frequency is increasing with increased ballast mass, even though
the mooring stiﬀness is reducing and mass (inertia) and added mass are increasing. The
hydrostatic stiﬀness in pitch is computed (in WAMIT) through [19]
chydro,33 = ρwgS11 + ρwgVdispzB −mf gzG
(3.10)
in which Vdisp is the displaced volume, zB is the centre of buoyancy, zG is the centre of
gravity and S11 is the waterplane moment expressed as
S11 =
Z Z
S
x1x1dS
(3.11)
Closer inspection reveals that despite the decrease in pitch mooring stiﬀness, the total
amount of stiﬀness in pitch, is actually increasing by a factor of almost 2.5 when going
from -20 % to 20 % ballast mass, hence increasing the natural frequency. The contribution
from the change in ﬂoater mass and centre of gravity, i.e. last term in eq. (3.10), is more
pronounced than the change in displaced volume and centre of buoyancy.
Nacelle acceleration
The nacelle acceleration for the static wind forcing is zero, naturally owing to the static
nature of the forcing.
The responses to wave forcing induce identical tendencies, but signiﬁcantly larger
magnitudes of acceleration for the focused wave. The reason being the introduction of
a sudden extreme wave, with no ramping of the surface elevation prior to wave impact.
Because the nacelle acceleration is inherently linked to the surge and pitch response
(actually the acceleration), and that these motions increase with increased ballast as
discussed, it comes as no surprise that the acceleration at the nacelle also increases with
increasing ballast.
The tower ﬂexibility only help to increase the magnitude of this
acceleration even further.
The tower natural frequency is observed to decrease with increasing ballast mass. Note
that the tower structural stiﬀness is not changed at any time during this or any subsequent
computations. Hence the reason for the lowering of the tower natural frequency must be
attributed to coupling eﬀects.

Chapter 3. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis and Parameter Constraints
3.1.2
Column Diameter
The sensitivity analysis on the column diameter has been conducted by varying the
diameter of the three columns simultaneously in the interval 13.5-16.5 m, in increments
of 0.3 m, with 15 m being the baseline value of the baseline TripleSpar.
The results from this analysis are shown on Figure 3.4. In Appendix E.2 the WAMIT
hydrodynamic properties and RAOs for the two outermost values in the investigated
column diameter interval may be consulted.
Surge motion
Increasing the column diameter yields lower surge responses from the static wind forcing.
When the column diameter is increased, the mass of the ﬂoater is increased as well due
to the extra material now present in the concrete columns. In addition however, this
increase in diameter also entails an increased displaced volume, which appears to be
more pronounced than the corresponding increase in mass, since an increase in freeboard
is actually observed. Owing to the increase in freeboard, the surge mooring stiﬀness is
also increased, due to a larger weight of mooring lines as these are now lifting from the
sea bed. As a result of this the wind response is decreasing.
Response due to wave forcing is seen to decrease slightly with increasing column
diameter.
As the ﬂoater columns are widened, and thus the area seen by the wave
forces gets larger, the hydrodynamic surge forcing is seen to increase slightly, despite
the simultaneous increase in freeboard. However, as mentioned previously, the surge
mooring stiﬀness is also increasing. Further, radiation damping and added mass are also
altered. These eﬀects combined with the corresponding increase in total ﬂoater mass is
suﬃcient in order to slightly lower the maximum wave response with increasing diameter.
The surge natural frequency is not altered signiﬁcantly. Closer inspection reveals a
slight but insigniﬁcant increase in frequency. Hence it can be concluded that the stiﬀness
as well as mass and added mass are changing/increasing somewhat proportionally.
Heave motion
The responses due to wave forcing is seen to increase with increasing diameter. As men-
tioned earlier the freeboard increases which entails a slight increase in heave mooring
stiﬀness also. However, as the bottom of the ﬂoater now sits higher in the water, the
vertical particle velocities are larger too. This combined with the simultaneous increase
in heave plate diameter (which is slaved by the column diameter) leads to larger forces,
and consequently responses as well. These eﬀects are more pronounced than the corre-
sponding increase in added mass and damping. As a ﬁnal comment it should be noted
that from the RAO plot, increasing the diameter moves the peak closer to the wave forcing
frequencies, which will also contribute to the larger responses.
The heave natural frequency is seen to increase with column diameter. Besides the
increasing heave mooring stiﬀness, an increase in hydrostatic stiﬀness also occurs as
==PAGE:67==
3.1. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis
43
dictated by [19]
chydro,22 = ρwgS
(3.12)
in which S is the waterplane area which naturally increases for larger column diameters.
The increase in total heave stiﬀness is thus more pronounced than the simultaneous
increase in mass and added mass.
Pitch motion
In pitch the static wind response is increasing with increasing diameter. The reason being
that despite the increase in pitch mooring stiﬀness, the torque arm increases as the hub
height is increased due to the signiﬁcantly larger freeboard. Further, the hydrostatic pitch
stiﬀness, eq. (3.10), is altered in every term and actually decreases overall. All of these
eﬀects yield a larger pitch response due to wind forcing.
The response due to wave forcing on the other hand actually decreases with increas-
ing diameter. As observed, not only the pitch mooring stiﬀness increases, so does the
hydrodynamic forcing. Furthermore, the pitch added mass, ﬂoater pitch inertia and total
stiﬀness are decreasing, while the damping on the contrary increases. Consequently, the
decrease in pitch response is diﬃcult to predict and may only be explained by the pitch
RAO, where the peak indeed is observed to move further away from the wave forcing
frequencies when increasing the column diameter.
Finally, the pitch natural frequency also decreases with increasing column diame-
ter. This indicates that the decrease in total pitch stiﬀness is more pronounced than the
concurrent decrease in pitch inertia and added mass.
Nacelle acceleration
For the wave forcing cases the nacelle acceleration closely follows the surge and pitch mo-
tion tendencies, since the nacelle acceleration is derived from the corresponding acceler-
ations as well as the tower ﬂexibility contribution. Consequently, the nacelle acceleration
decreases with increasing diameter.
The tower natural frequency is not observed to change signiﬁcantly. Detailed investi-
gations reveal a small almost negligible increase which is attributed to coupling eﬀects.
