3.1. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis
45
3.1.3
Column Spacing
The sensitivity analysis on the column spacing has been conducted by varying the spacing
measured from the center of the ﬂoater, to the center of each of the three columns, in the
interval 16 m to 36 m, in increments of 2 m, with 26 m being the baseline value.
The results from this analysis are shown on Figure 3.6. In Appendix E.3 the WAMIT
hydrodynamic properties and RAOs for the two outermost values in the investigated
column spacing interval may be consulted.
Surge motion
When increasing the column spacing a remarkable drop in surge mooring stiﬀness is
observed. This leads to a large increase in surge response due to static wind forcing. As
the column spacing is increased, the fairleads are pushed further outwards resulting in
a more slack mooring line with reduced stiﬀness as a result (more of the mooring line
now rests on the sea bed). Also the draught is increased slightly due to a small increase
in ﬂoater mass stemming from the extra material now present in the arms of the ﬂoater
which connects the column. This will also induce slightly lower mooring stiﬀness.
For the focused wave and regular wave forcing, a decrease in response with increasing
column spacing is observed, despite the decrease in surge mooring stiﬀness. It is noted
that the surge hydrodynamic forcing is decreasing with increasing column spacing. Es-
pecially relevant when varying the column spacing are the eﬀects of wave addition and
cancellation. In this work the wave propagation is aligned with the x-axis, hence when
considering the ﬂoater in the xz-plane, it can be said to consist of two columns aligned
with the direction of wave propagation. The eﬀect of wave addition occurs when a prop-
agating wave has a wave length matching the distance between the two columns. Here
wave crests (and also troughs) will occur at each column simultaneously, leading to large
wave loads since each column will experience the full force of the wave. In contrary, wave
cancellation occurs when the distance between the column are half the wave length of
the propagating wave. In this case a crest and trough will occur simultaneously at each
column respectively, leading to a lower resulting wave load on the ﬂoater. A sketch of the
two phenomena is shown on Figure 3.5.
Consider a column spacing of 36 m. The wave-aligned distance from the centre of the
ﬁrst columns to the centre of the back column is 54 m. Looking at the surge excitation
forces in Appendix E.3 for a column spacing of 36 m, reveals a large force peak at ω = 0.967
rad/s. Solving the linear dispersion relation, i.e. eq. 2.32, on 180 m water depth yields a
wave length of λ = 66 m, which is not far from the 54 m. Hence as the column spacing is
close to the wave length, a large degree of wave addition must occur at this point leading
to large forces. Contrary, the low force trough at ω = 1.344 rad/s corresponds to a wave
length of λ = 34 m. Consequently, as 3λ/2 = 51 m, which indeed is very close to 54 m,
means that wave cancellation eﬀects are present here. Naturally, depending on the wave
length for these occurrences, the magnitude of the excitation force will also vary with the
==PAGE:70==
46
Chapter 3. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis and Parameter Constraints
λ
(a) Wave addition.
λ/2
(b) Wave cancellation.
Figure 3.5: Sketch showing the principles of wave addition and wave cancellation.
column spacing. Besides this phenomenon, diﬀraction eﬀects will also occur. This means
that as the column spacing is reduced, the incoming waves will "see" a larger portion
of the ﬂoater more spatially conﬁned, thus yielding larger diﬀraction forces.
When
on the other hand increasing the spacing, the incoming waves will to a larger extend
"see" three relatively solitary cylinders, which will results in lower diﬀraction forces.
This explains why the surge hydrodynamic forcing is decreasing with increased column
spacing. Besides this decrease in hydrodynamic forcing, the added mass is increased and
the damping altered as well.
Lastly, the surge natural frequency is seen to decrease with increasing column spacing.
The reason for this decrease is the decreasing surge mooring stiﬀness, combined with the
additional ﬂoater mass and added mass, owing to the larger dimensions and resulting
larger draught, when increasing the column spacing.
Heave motion
It is seen that the response due to wave forcing is decreasing with increased column
spacing. When the column spacing, and thus also ﬂoater mass, is increased, the mooring
line will become more slack because the fairleads are pushed further out and down.
Consequently, the heave mooring stiﬀness is reduced while the hydrostatic stiﬀness is
also altered as the displaced volume etc. are slightly changing as well. Further, a decrease
in heave hydrodynamic forcing is observed. A decrease might be expected due to the
increased draught which will result in smaller magnitudes of vertical particle velocities.
Additionally, increasing the spacing also lowers the heave added mass signiﬁcantly, since
increasing the spacing lowers the amount of water accelerated with the ﬂoater motions.
The resulting eﬀect of these changing parameters is a decrease in response, where it may be
assumed that the most pronounced cause for this is the decreasing heave hydrodynamic
forcing.
It is noted that the heave natural frequency remains virtually unchanged, indicating a
==PAGE:71==
3.1. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis
47
balance between the total stiﬀness in heave, and the decreasing added mass and increasing
ﬂoater mass.
Pitch motion
The pitch response to the static wind forcing is seen to decrease remarkably with in-
creasing column spacing. It is observed that the increase in spacing does not alter the
pitch mooring stiﬀness signiﬁcantly. However, the hydrostatic pitch stiﬀness increases
signiﬁcantly due to the larger dimensions, consequently reducing the pitch response.
The pitch response to wave forcing is also decreasing with increased spacing, due to
the aforementioned causes concerning diﬀraction etc. plus the higher pitch added mass
and inertia. While the pitch RAO peak shifts slightly towards the higher-frequency range,
the lower hydrodynamic forcing is more pronounced and lower responses are observed.
Finally, the pitch natural frequency is observed to increase with increasing spacing.
As mentioned the pitch mooring stiﬀness is actually reduced slightly. More detailed
investigations however reveal that the total pitch stiﬀness is increased when increasing
the spacing. In fact, the total pitch stiﬀness is increasing by a factor of almost 2.2 going
from 16 m to 36 m spacing. However, the added mass and pitch inertia is also seen to
increase in the same interval. Consequently it can be deduced that the increase in total
pitch stiﬀness is most pronounced as the frequency is observed to increase.
Nacelle acceleration
For wave forcing on the other hand, a decreasing nacelle acceleration is observed when
increasing the spacing. As for the prior conducted analyses, the nacelle acceleration is
linked to the surge and pitch response, and hence as these decrease, the nacelle accelera-
tion is expected, and indeed seen, to decrease with increasing spacing as well.
It is noted that the tower natural frequency decreases for increased column spacing.
This decrease is due to coupling eﬀects.

3.1.4
Column Length
The sensitivity analysis on the column length has been conducted by varying the column
length in the interval 52.5 m to 77.5, in increments of 2.5 m, with 65 m begin the baseline
value.
The results from this analysis are shown on Figure 3.7. In Appendix E.4 the WAMIT
hydrodynamic properties and RAOs for the two outermost values in the investigated
column length interval may be consulted.
Surge motion
Increasing the length of the columns yields smaller surge responses due to the static wind
forcing. When the length is increased, the freeboard is observed to increase signiﬁcantly.
This will entail increased stiﬀness in surge from the mooring lines, consequently reducing
the response, even despite the larger torque arm also occurring.
Considering the response due to wave forcing, it is observed that these responses
also decrease with increasing column length, however to a lesser extent. As mentioned
previously, the surge mooring stiﬀness is increasing. However, it is also observed that the
surge hydrodynamic forcing is increasing slightly. Increasing the column length not only
results in increased freeboard, the draught will also increase slightly. As a consequence
the area seen by the horizontal particle velocities becomes larger, hence this increase in
forcing occurs. Lastly, the total ﬂoater mass and added mass increase with increasing
length.
In the end the increasing mooring stiﬀness, mass and added mass are more
pronounced than the increasing hydrodynamic forcing, and a decreasing response is
observed.
Concerning the surge natural frequency, a very small decrease in frequency with
increased column length is observed. The surge stiﬀness and inertia thus change almost
proportionately.
Heave motion
The response due to wave forcing is observed to decrease with increasing column length.
The heave mooring stiﬀness is increasing slightly with increased column length as seen.
In addition, the heave hydrodynamic forcing is decreasing with increasing column length,
due to the simultaneous increase in ﬂoater draught. In addition to this, the total mass
of the ﬂoater also increases contributing to lowering the response as well. Finally, it is
observed from the heave RAO that increasing the column length, shifts the peak further
away from the wave forcing frequencies. All in all this results in the lower heave responses
observed.
The heave natural frequency is decreasing slightly with increasing length. Despite
the increase in heave mooring stiﬀness when increasing the column length (hydrostatic
remains unchanged), the corresponding increase in ﬂoater mass, is more pronounced in
this case.
==PAGE:74==
50
Chapter 3. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis and Parameter Constraints
Pitch motion
In pitch the response due to the static wind forcing is observed to increase with increasing
column length. It is noted that the pitch mooring stiﬀness is increasing with increased
column length. However, due to the signiﬁcant increase in freeboard the torque on the
ﬂoater is also increasing signiﬁcantly. Furthermore, due to the varying column length, the
pitch hydrostatic stiﬀness also changes. Closer inspections reveal that the total stiﬀness
in pitch is reduced by a factor of 1.3 going from 52.5 m to 77.5 m column length. This
consequently explains the larger static pitch response.
Responses due to wave forcing are on the other hand decreasing when increasing the
column length. Increasing column length yields a larger draught which in turn yields
larger pitch hydrodynamic forcing, as is also the case for the WAMIT excitation forces.
Increasing column length also results in increased added mass and radiation damping.
Combining this with the simultaneous increase in pitch inertia is thus more pronounced,
to overcome the concurrent increase in hydrodynamic forcing and the lower total pitch
stiﬀness, resulting in lower responses. Also contributing signiﬁcantly to this is the shift
of the pitch RAO peak towards lower frequencies and hence away from the wave forcing
frequencies.
Finally, the pitch natural frequency is decreasing with increasing column length. As
the total pitch stiﬀness is decreasing, and inertia and added mass are increasing with
length, it is no surprise that the natural frequency drops with increasing length.
Nacelle acceleration
The nacelle acceleration induced by the waves are seen to decrease with increasing column
length. Due to the dependency of the nacelle acceleration on the surge and pitch motion
it is expected, and also observed, that the nacelle acceleration decreases with increasing
column length.
The tower natural frequency is observed to decrease with length. This is attributed to
coupling eﬀects.

3.1.5
Mooring Line Length
The sensitivity analysis on the mooring line length has been conducted by varying the
length in the interval 580 m to 640 m, in increments of 6 m, with 610 m being the baseline
value. The lengths of the three mooring lines have been varied simultaneously. It must
be emphasised that in these calculations the weight of the mooring lines have not been
included when determining the total mass of the ﬂoater. Hence, as modelled here they are
for simplicity not contributing to changing draught, only changing mooring line stiﬀness.
On Figure 3.8 the results from this sensitivity analysis are shown. In Appendix E.5
the WAMIT hydrodynamic properties and RAOs for the two outermost values in the
investigated mooring line length interval may be consulted. Note that as the draught and
other ﬂoater properties are not aﬀected during this analysis, the WAMIT results will be
identical for the two outermost values. They are only included here for completeness.
Surge motion
Increasing the mooring line length, entails a remarkable increase in surge response due
to the static wind forcing. It is seen that the surge mooring stiﬀness decreases when
increasing the line length, hence an increase in response is expected.
As the line is
lengthened, the tension in its drops, and more of the line gets to rest on the sea bed.
The response due to wave forcing is more or less constant for all lengths investigated.
The reason why the response seems unaﬀected may be because the ﬂoater itself is not
shifted far enough away from its equilibrium position, to make the changing mooring line
stiﬀness provide a signiﬁcant eﬀect on the motion restriction. Another factor which may
contribute to this can be seen on the surge RAO. Here the peaks shifts further away from
the wave forcing frequencies, thus yielding potentially lower responses. The combination
of the lower mooring stiﬀness, and shifting of RAO peak, may thus cancel out yielding a
constant response in this case.
Reducing the stiﬀness by increasing the line length naturally results in lower natural
frequencies, as neither mass nor added mass are altered in this case.
Heave motion
Regarding the response to wave forcing a very small, almost negligible, decrement is
observed when increasing the mooring line length. The heave mooring stiﬀness is re-
ducing with increased mooring length, albeit not as much as the surge or pitch stiﬀness.
Further, the heave RAO is not shifting much away from the wave forcing frequencies,
hence again the ﬂoater may not be displaced suﬃciently from its equilibrium position to
see a diﬀerence, despite the lower stiﬀness.
Due to the decreasing heave stiﬀness a slight lowering of the heave natural frequency
entails.
==PAGE:77==
3.1. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis
53
Pitch motion
In pitch the response to the static wind forcing is increasing with increased mooring line
length, again due to the lower pitch mooring stiﬀness.
The response to wave forcing on the other hand is observed to decrease with increas-
ing line length, despite the lower stiﬀness. Again, observing the pitch RAO, the peak
is shifting further away from the wave forcing frequencies, thus allowing lower pitch
responses when increasing the line length.
Owing to the decreasing stiﬀness, the pitch natural frequency is decreasing with
increasing mooring line length.
Nacelle acceleration
The response to wave forcing is in surge seen to be almost constant (a small decrement
may be observed) and is in pitch seen to decrease, hence the nacelle acceleration due to
wave forcing must decrease as well, which is also observed.
The tower natural frequency is observed to decrease slightly, which is attributed to
coupling eﬀects.

==PAGE:79==
3.1. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis
55
3.1.6
Mooring Line Mass
The sensitivity analysis on the mooring line mass has been conducted by varying the
mass between -50 % to +50 % of the amount originally used in the baseline TripleSpar
mooring system, in increments of 10 %. The mass of each of the three mooring lines
has been varied simultaneously. It must again in this case as for the previous case be
emphasised that in the calculations the actual weight of the mooring lines have not been
included when determining the total mass of the ﬂoater. Consequently, only the stiﬀness
of the mooring lines change and not draught and so forth.
On Figure 3.9 the results from this sensitivity analysis are shown. In Appendix E.6
the WAMIT hydrodynamic properties and RAOs for the two outermost values in the
investigated mooring line mass interval may be consulted. Note that as the draught is not
aﬀected during this analysis, the WAMIT results will be identical for the two outermost
values and are only included here for completeness.
Surge motion
In surge the response due to the static wind forcing is observed to decrease with increasing
line mass. As the mass of the mooring lines are increased, the stiﬀness will increase as
well, due to the added weight pulling at the fairleads. As a consequence of this increased
stiﬀness, the response is decreasing.
Concerning wave forcing the response is not altered noticeably. While as the mooring
mass is increased, the peak in the surge RAO is shifting further towards the wave forcing
frequencies, potentially inducing larger responses. As for the prior case however, the
ﬂoater may not be displaced suﬃciently from its equilibrium position order to see this
eﬀect.
Increasing the stiﬀness in surge obviously entails increasing surge natural frequencies,
as the inertia of the ﬂoater remains unchanged.
Heave motion
The wave response is seen to increase slightly with increasing line mass. Increasing line
mass shifts the heave RAO peak slightly (almost negligible) towards the wave forcing
frequencies, hence despite the increase in stiﬀness, a very small increase in response is
observed still. The heave natural frequency increases very little, owing to the increasing
stiﬀness.
Apparently, the heave natural frequency is not really governed by the mooring system
stiﬀness, but instead of the heave hydrostatic stiﬀness (which remains unchanged in this
case).
Pitch motion
In pitch the response to the static wind forcing is decreasing with increasing line mass,
due to the corresponding increase in pitch mooring stiﬀness.
==PAGE:80==
56
Chapter 3. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis and Parameter Constraints
Again, the response to wave forcing is increasing slightly with increased line mass
despite the increase in stiﬀness, because the increasing mass shifts the pitch RAO peak
further towards the wave forcing frequencies.
Owing to the increasing stiﬀness the pitch natural frequency also increases for heavier
mooring lines.
Nacelle acceleration
The wave induced nacelle accelerations increase slightly with increasing line mass. The
surge response was basically constant, while it was seen that in pitch the responses
increased slightly with increasing mass. Hence the small increase in nacelle acceleration
due to wave forcing is indeed expected.
The tower natural frequency is virtually constant.

Having performed a sensitivity analysis and investigated the eﬀects of the six chosen input

qualitatively. In order to facilitate easier access to these results at a later stage, without
having to go and look into each ﬁgure every time, a summative overview is provided in
Table 3.2. The aim with this table is to provide the reader with a quick and relatively
easy way to investigate what the eﬀect of one of the input parameters has on the system
response, in each DoF.

While it seems almost logical that e.g. the ballast mass has a large eﬀect on the static pitch
response and natural frequency, due to the lower centre of gravity and corresponding
change in inertia. And that the column spacing has a large eﬀect on the static pitch
response due to the larger dimensions, some connections are not that intuitive.
For
instance, one might imagine that changing the column diameters would yield a change
in the surge natural frequency, this is however seen to not be the case. Further, while the
mooring line length and mass signiﬁcantly alters the surge characteristics, they are more
or less redundant in modifying characteristics in heave. This however may be due to the
negligence of mooring line mass on total system mass.
Thus a sensitivity analysis as the one conducted here, can play an important part
in establishing connections between parameters and to clarify which ones ought to be
investigated in further detail.
Consequently, spending time on a sensitivity analysis,
==PAGE:83==
3.2. TripleSpar Constraints and Design Limits
59
despite the initial time spent on it, may in the end provide a more eﬃcient and hence
cheaper way to design a ﬂoater, since redundant system parameters can be excluded from
further investigations, leading to a more feasible design process and hopefully a better
design in the end.
3.2
TripleSpar Constraints and Design Limits
Once the results from the sensitivity analysis have been investigated, the constraints or
design limits, i.e. upper and lower bounds on the input parameters, can be established.
When designing ﬂoating substructures in general, it is desired to have the natural
frequencies located away from frequency regions were either wind or wave forcing may
be present.
Considering wind turbines, 1P and 3P frequencies are also introduced,
which should also not coincide with the ﬂoater natural frequencies. The 1P frequency
corresponds to the rotational speed of the rotor and c.f. Table 2.2 lies in the interval [0.10
0.16] Hz for the DTU 10 MW RWT. The 3P frequency is the blade passing frequency and
may introduce oscillations as the blades momentarily shadows for the tower when they
pass by (3-bladed turbine). The 3P frequency lies in the interval [0.30 0.48] Hz. If the
natural frequencies coincide with a frequency where forcing is applied, resonance may
occur which can lead to large unwanted responses and an increase in rate of fatigue for
structural components.
In addition to these frequency constraints, certain constraints on the pitch response
and nacelle acceleration are also introduced. The reason for these constraints is that as the
ﬂoater, and thus also the nacelle, pitches, uneven loads occur at the attachment point of the
hub to the nacelle, which causes increased wear and tear of bearings etc. during active
operation. Also the eﬃciency and power output of the wind turbine might decrease.
Further, limits to the fore-aft acceleration of the nacelle have also been established, such
that the structural components of the RNA are not taking unwanted damage.
In the present study the constraints listed in Table 3.3 have been proposed by co-
supervisor Henrik Stiesdal.
Table 3.3: Floater constraints for pitch motion and nacelle acceleration, under operational
conditions and when the turbine is parked/non-operational.
Operating condition
Response constraint
Pitch [deg]
Nac. acc. [m/s2]
Operational
6
0.3 g
Non-operational
10
0.5 g
Based on these constraints the pitch and nacelle acceleration results from the previous
sensitivity analysis have been extended to include these constraints. Further, a ﬁgure
containing the frequency constraints has been produced for visualisation purposes. Con-
sequently, a ﬁgure containing the frequency and response constraints has been produced
for each of the six investigated input parameters. These ﬁgures are to be interpreted the
==PAGE:84==
60
Chapter 3. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis and Parameter Constraints
following way; the ﬁrst subﬁgure shows the frequency constraints with the wind PSD,
1P- and 3P frequency bands, wave forcing frequency band (assuming wave periods in the
range [3 20] s) and natural frequencies plotted. Note that the natural frequencies are each
plotted with two vertical bars, which represent the value of the natural frequency for the
lowest and highest input parameter value, respectively. The arrows plotted above each
natural frequency gap indicates in which direction the natural frequency shifts when in-
creasing the value of the input parameter. The next two subﬁgures show the same results
as those shown earlier on the results for the sensitivity analysis in the previous section.
However, horizontal lines have in this case been added to the plots in order to illustrate
the pitch response and nacelle acceleration constraints as listed in Table 3.3. The ﬁgures
thus aim to visualise whether or not the present constraints are exceeded for certain input
parameter values, in a relatively simple manner.
3.2.1
Ballast Mass
A visualisation of the constraints when varying the ballast mass is seen on Figure 3.10. It is
observed that the pitch and tower natural frequencies are altered the most, with the heave
natural frequency to a lesser extent and the surge natural frequency even less. Increasing
the ballast mass shifts the pitch natural frequency closer to the wave forcing frequency
band, while the heave natural frequency shifts further away though still located inside
the wave band.
The pitch operational limit is observed to be exceeded for the static wind forcing
when removing more than about 5 % of the original ballast mass. The responses due
to wave forcing stay beneath the operational limit for all values considered here. The
non-operational pitch limit is not exceeded for any case.
The operational as well as non-operational limit for the nacelle acceleration is exceeded
for the focused wave forcing for all values considered, whereas the regular wave forcing
exceeds neither limit.
3.2.2
Column Diameter
The results of varying the column diameter in relation to the constraints are visualised
on Figure 3.11. The pitch natural frequency is most susceptible to the varying column
diameter, with the heave frequency also being very sensitive. The surge and tower natural
frequencies on the other hand are not altered signiﬁcantly. With increasing diameter, the
heave natural frequency moves further into the wave frequency band, while the pitch
natural frequency moves away. Concerning the pitch constraints, the operational limit
is exceeded for the static wind forcing when increasing the diameter to 15.75 m, while
responses due to wave forcing are not exceeding either limit. Regular wave forcing does
not yield nacelle accelerations above the operational limit. However, focused wave forcing
induces large nacelle accelerations which are above even the non-operational limit.

3.2.3
Column Spacing
The constraints when varying the column spacing is shown on Figure 3.12. It is observed
that the tower natural frequency is heavily inﬂuenced. The pitch, surge and subsequently
heave natural frequencies are altered to a much lesser extent. The pitch natural frequency
shifts further towards the wave forcing frequency band, while the surge natural frequency
shifts towards the wind forcing, when increasing the spacing.
Lowering the column spacing to ∼23 m or less entails pitch responses above the
operational limit for static wind forcing. The responses due to wave forcing do not exceed
the operational limit for all column spacings investigated.
The nacelle acceleration constraints are only exceeded for the focused wave forcing,
where it is for the lowest spacing seen to be far beyond the non-operational limit as well.
3.2.4
Column Length
On Figure 3.13 the constraints when varying the column length may be observed. Again,
the tower natural frequency is altered the most, albeit not as much as for column spacing,
with pitch natural frequency also being altered signiﬁcantly. The heave natural frequency
is changed less while the surge natural frequency remains virtually unchanged. For both
heave and pitch increasing the column length shifts the frequencies further away from
the wave forcing frequency band.
Increasing the column length beyond 74 m yields pitch responses, due to static wind
forcing, above the operational limit.
Wave forcing does not result in too high pitch
responses on the other hand.
The nacelle acceleration constraints are only exceeded for the focused wave forcing,
which exceeds the non-operational limit for all lengths considered.

3.2.5
Mooring Line Length
Varying the mooring line length entails constraints as shown on Figure 3.14. The surge
and pitch natural frequencies are altered the most, while the heave and tower natural

In pitch neither the static wind forcing not the two diﬀerent wave forcing yield re-

The nacelle acceleration constraint is only exceeded for the focused wave forcing which
is seen to exceed the non-operational limit.

Just as for the actual sensitivity analysis, a summative table of the ﬁndings from the
design limit investigation may be seen in Table 3.4, in order to provide an easy access
to the ﬁndings. The table provides an overview of whether or not the range of input
parameter value chosen for the sensitivity analysis, complies with the constraints in both
operational and non-operational conditions. Where applicable, the limiting value of a
given input parameter needed to comply with a certain constraint, is further listed.

It is noted that the static wind pitch response is most susceptible to varying input parame-
ters, in relation to the chosen constraints for the operational condition. The hydrodynamic
forcing complies with the constraints for the pitch operational condition, while concern-
ing the operational nacelle acceleration constraint, only the regular wave response stays
within the constraint limits. For the non-operational condition all pitch constraints are
complied with, however concerning nacelle acceleration, the focused wave once more
proves to exceed that constraint.
Consequently, it is clear to see that the focused wave severely causes diﬃculties for
the ﬂoater to comply with the nacelle acceleration constraints. The baseline TripleSpar
==PAGE:94==
70
Chapter 3. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis and Parameter Constraints
ﬂoater might not be designed to withstand a force such as a focused wave, therefore the
violation of the constraints for all values of the various input parameters. However, the
purpose of this investigation is indeed to clarify such limitations in relation to a proposed
design, such that potentially a revised design that complies with the focused wave forcing
may be drawn. Naturally, this constraint investigation is a necessity, since for the purpose
of designing a ﬂoater, it does not make any sense to conduct a sensitivity analysis without
also conducting a subsequent investigation into the constraints. Academically however,
a sensitivity analysis may be conducted on its own, to provide augmented knowledge of
parameters, as outlined in the beginning of this chapter.
3.3
Alternative TripleSpar Substructure Conﬁgurations
The sensitivity analysis and the parameter design limit investigation have resulted in
increased knowledge of the input parameters and how they individually aﬀect the re-
sponses and natural frequencies in each DoF. As a natural extension to these analyses,
it would be interesting to conceive two alternative designs to the original TripleSpar
ﬂoater, in order to extend the exploration of the design space further. Using the original
TripleSpar ﬂoater as a starting point for this design process, the two alternative designs
may be chosen as
1. one with a relatively large waterplane area and shallow draught
2. one with a relatively small waterplane area and deep draught
The original baseline TripleSpar ﬂoater thus lies somewhere in between these two new
designs.
As a slight digression, the new designs consider compliance in relation to
frequency-, pitch and nacelle acceleration constraints as previously outlined. Therefore,
further design iterations may optimise on other ﬂoater properties subsequently.
The new designs are subjected to the same environmental conditions as used in the
TripleSpar sensitivity analysis listed in Table 3.1, and are also located on 180 m water
depth.
3.3.1
Description of Alternative TripleSpar Designs
It was out of interest decided that the new design having a large waterplane area, would
have a column spacing of double that of the original TripleSpar ﬂoater, while the ﬂoater
with the small waterplane area would have half that. Consequently, the ﬁrst new alter-
native to the original TripleSpar design consists of a relatively shallow draught ﬂoater
with increased water plane area and larger diameter columns. The second new alter-
native consists of a relatively deep draught ﬂoater with a corresponding smaller water
plane area and smaller column diameter. Relevant parameters for the two new designs
are outlined in Table 3.5. For comparison the original TripleSpar conﬁguration has been
included as well. The new designs are additionally sketched on Figure 3.16. Note that
==PAGE:95==
3.3. Alternative TripleSpar Substructure Conﬁgurations
71
structural properties, i.e. stresses and strains etc., have been neglected in the present
study, but would naturally be very important in an actual detailed design scheme.

As stated previously, the new designs are exposed to the same environmental conditions

results are presented in a similar manner as for the earlier conducted constraints investi-

New design #1
The new design #1 was subjected to 12 design iterations with updated WAMIT input for
each iteration. From Figure 3.12 it is noted that increasing the column spacing yields
higher pitch natural frequencies and signiﬁcantly lower tower natural frequencies. Fur-
ther, on Figure 3.13 it is seen that decreasing the column length results in higher pitch,
heave and tower natural frequencies. Lastly, increasing the column diameter as seen on
Figure 3.11 on the other hand results in lower pitch natural frequency and higher heave
natural frequency. Thus increasing the column spacing and subsequently tuning the nat-
ural frequencies is as a result, a delicate balance between these parameters and requires
several design iterations. After the ﬁrst few iterations it was decided not to reduce the
column length below 40 m, in order to keep a balance between the corresponding in-
creasing pitch natural frequency, while at the same time maintaining a relatively shallow
draught. Reducing the column length lowers the freeboard owing to less displaced vol-
ume as shown on Figure 3.7, while increasing the column diameter however signiﬁcantly
increases the freeboard c.f. Figure 3.4. When varying the column diameter it is hence
necessary to also alter the ballast mass, in order to maintain a hub height of 119 m, for
a constant column length. On Figure 3.10 it is noted that increasing the ballast mass
increases the pitch natural frequency and lowers the heave natural frequency. Having
ﬁxed the column spacing and length, while for a moment neglecting the mooring lines,
a situation where it is necessary to increase the column diameter in order to provide suf-
ﬁcient buoyancy, while at the same time balancing this parameter with increasing ballast
mass to provide the correct hub height exists. It is noted on Figure 3.10 and 3.11 that the
heave and pitch natural frequencies counteract each other for increasing parameter value.
During the design iterations what could best be described as a ’spiral of ever in-
creasing dimensions’ was recorded as follows. Increasing the column diameter to yield
lower pitch natural frequencies also results in higher freeboard. Next, increasing the
ballast mass to lower the freeboard to maintain a correct hub height conversely results
in higher pitch natural frequencies. This meant that it was necessary to increase the
column diameter once more, and the process would then repeat itself, leading to ever
==PAGE:97==
3.3. Alternative TripleSpar Substructure Conﬁgurations
73
increasing dimensions for just minor incremental changes in pitch and heave natural fre-
quencies. Naturally, such a potentially never ending spiral of ever increasing dimensions
ought to be avoided, hence it was decided to stop the design process after 12 itera-
tions, since the ﬂoater was gradually becoming way too massive to be a feasible design.
Figure 3.17: Steel-intensive MHI 7 MW ﬂoater
from Fukushima FORWARD [27].
This phenomenon of ever increasing di-
mensions is discussed by [7] to possibly
explain one of the reasons for the often
very large ﬂoating concepts coming from
Japan, such as the ﬂoater visualised on Fig-
ure 3.17.
Finally, while increasing the mooring
line length also yields a lowering of the
pitch natural frequency as observed on
Figure 3.14, it also signiﬁcantly lowers the
surge mooring stiﬀness, and as a result po-
tentially allows for very large and undesir-
able static wind surge responses. The low-
ering of the mooring line length to 580 m
must be viewed in relation to the increase
in ﬂoater radius i.e.
increase in column
spacing, hence the net eﬀect of this almost diminishes in this case. Further, increasing
mooring line length allows for larger static wind pitch response, which should naturally
be avoided. Lastly, lower mooring line mass also yields lower natural frequencies, but
should again be viewed in relation to the static surge and pitch responses, and further
the eﬀect is not as pronounced as when modifying other ﬂoater parameters.
Observing the results on Figure 3.18, the heave and pitch natural frequencies are
located within the wave forcing frequency band, due to the termination of the iterative
design process. They will however potentially only be excited by rather severe sea states.
When operating at rated wind speed, which the turbine must be assumed to do most of
the time, the 3P frequency must be around 0.48 Hz, hence shifting the tower frequency
away from this frequency is beneﬁcial. As expected the static wind pitch response is very
limited and is around 0.6 deg when exposed to 2Frated, due to the signiﬁcant increase in
pitch hydrostatic stiﬀness owing to the wider columns as expressed by eq. (3.10). Con-
sidering the nacelle acceleration, it is noted that the response due to regular wave forcing
just barely stays within the operational constraint, while the response due to focused
wave forcing just barely stays within the non-operational constraint. Cf. Table 3.4 this is
the ﬁrst time the non-operational nacelle acceleration constraint is complied with. The
static wind surge response of 33.5 m is observed to immediately look rather extreme,
owing to the decreasing surge mooring stiﬀness. Considering the fact that the baseline
TripleSpar ﬂoater has a static wind surge response of 28 m as seen on e.g. Figure 3.3, this
is however not that critical after all - the surge response due to wave forcing is indeed
==PAGE:98==
74
Chapter 3. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis and Parameter Constraints
also much less than the static response. Lastly, the heave response is larger than for the
baseline TripleSpar owing to the shallower draught, which allows for higher vertical wave
kinematics as well as the wider columns and heave plates resulting in a larger area.
New design #2
The new design #2 was subjected to 7 design iterations with updated WAMIT input
for each iteration as well. Cf. Figure 3.12 lowering the column spacing entails lower
pitch and higher tower natural frequencies. Further, increasing the column length will
result in lower pitch and heave natural frequencies. While the column length for design
#1 was shortened 25 m compared to the baseline TripleSpar, it was decided to increase
the column length by 25 m for design #2 compared to the baseline TripleSpar, thus again
eliminating the column length as a decision variable. A smaller column diameter is on the
other hand seen on Figure 3.11 to yield a higher pitch natural frequency and lower heave
natural frequency. Again, initially neglecting the mooring lines, increasing the column
length to 90 m and concurrently reducing the column diameter entails a reduction in
net buoyancy, as the contribution from the change in diameter is more pronounced than
the contribution from changing column length in this case. As a result it was necessary
to reduce the amount of ballast mass as well to achieve the desired hub height, which
on Figure 3.10 is observed to entail lower pitch and higher heave natural frequencies.
Inﬂuenced by the increase in column length and resulting decrease in pitch and heave
natural frequencies, it was after a few iterations possible to shift the heave and pitch
natural frequencies towards the lower edge of the wave forcing frequency band.
Due to the initially favourable heave and pitch natural frequencies, it was for this
particular design investigated how much the static surge motion could be limited by
modifying the mooring system, while still having acceptable heave and pitch natural
frequencies. On Figure 3.14 and 3.15 it is observed that shortening the mooring line length
yields higher restoring stiﬀness in each DoF, and thus higher natural frequencies. Using a
lighter mooring line reduces the restoring stiﬀness and consequently the frequencies. For
the values considered here, modifying the line length entails the most signiﬁcant change.
Thus in order to be able to increase the surge mooring stiﬀness the most, the mooring
line length should be reduced (note that the ﬂoater radius i.e. column spacing has also
been reduced). While doing so yields a stiﬀer mooring system, the natural frequencies
are also increased, hence it was necessary to decrease the mooring line mass slightly in
order to obtain the desired heave and pitch natural frequencies outside the wave forcing
frequency band.
On Figure 3.18 a higher static wind pitch response is observed, indicating that the
total pitch stiﬀness for design #2 is not as high as for design #1, which, despite the
stiﬀer mooring system, is caused by narrower columns yielding a smaller hydrostatic
stiﬀness as dictated by eq. (3.10). While the regular wave induced nacelle acceleration
is comparable to design #1, the focused wave response is seen to be much larger, by far
exceeding the non-operational limit just as for the baseline TripleSpar ﬂoater. Despite a
==PAGE:99==
3.3. Alternative TripleSpar Substructure Conﬁgurations
75
slight increase in overall pitch inertia, the reducing pitch stiﬀness allows for an increase
in focused wave pitch response. Further, the overall reduced ﬂoater mass yields a larger
response in surge compared to design #1, which means that larger nacelle accelerations
will occur. It is observed that it was possible to remarkably limit the static wind response
in surge, by modifying the mooring system appropriately. The static wind surge response
is even smaller than the regular wave induced surge response. Finally, owing to the deep
draught and small cross sectional area of the ﬂoater columns, the heave response is
basically insigniﬁcant for this design proposal.

Chapter 3. TripleSpar Sensitivity Analysis and Parameter Constraints
While the two designs proposed in this subsection are not feasible owing to a large mass
and draught respectively, the interesting phenomenon of ever increasing dimensions was
encountered. Thus, not having suﬃcient knowledge of the various input parameters
and their inﬂuence on the system dynamics, may result in the designer going in circles
in relation to optimising, as e.g. in this case, natural frequencies. As a consequence
the dimensions of the ﬂoater tends to grow, eventually becoming way to infeasible and
further wasting resources which in stead ought to be used on more feasible designs and
optimising on those instead. This underlines once more the importance of conducting
sensitivity analyses of various extent, when commencing a design process for a new
ﬂoater.
==PAGE:101==
Chapter4
TetraSpar Model Adaptation, System
Identiﬁcation and Optimal Design
In this chapter results from the TetraSpar type ﬂoater investigations are presented. Ini-
tially, a description and main ideas of the TetraSpar concept are provided.
Next, an
account on how the QuLAF numerical model has been extended and modiﬁed to con-
sider the new ﬂoater concept is given. After that, a system identiﬁcation on the novel
application of a suspended ballast, when varying the ballast mass and ballast depth,
is conducted. This system identiﬁcation is conducted for two diﬀerent ﬂoater conﬁg-
urations; one consisting of a semisub conﬁguration and another consisting of a spar
conﬁguration.
Finally, a TetraSpar design which is compliant with site conditions at
Buchan Deep, Scotland, is proposed, by considering a set of decision variables or input
parameters determined from the previous TripleSpar sensitivity analysis.
4.1
The TetraSpar Floating Foundation Concept
The TetraSpar ﬂoating foundation is an open-source concept conceived by co-supervisor
Henrik Stiesdal, with the aim to drive down the cost of ﬂoating wind, by virtue of
combining the best features of the spar, semisub and TLP concepts. C.f. Table 1.1 this
is sought achieved by combining all the advantages of the spar, while incorporating
the ﬂexible water depth application of the semisub and its ability for towing after e.g.
onshore/quayside assembly. Further, it also tries to incorporate the low weight of the
TLP, in order to bring down the amount of material used, and thus also the cost. The
proposed concept is a simple tetrahedral structure connected by cylindrical braces, with
an external suspended ballasted keel attached to the ﬂoater via e.g. steel chains. The
main buoyancy of the ﬂoater is provided through externally attached air-ﬁlled tanks. It is
important to note that [7] remarks that the TetraSpar concept is not necessarily a speciﬁc
design, but rather an approach towards designing more economically feasible ﬂoating
substructures, by utilising means of industrialised processes and techniques. Illustrations
of the concept are shown on Figure 4.1.
77
==PAGE:102==
78
Chapter 4. TetraSpar Model Adaptation, System Identiﬁcation and Optimal Design
(a) Overall geometric appearance.
(b) Installed with suspended keel.
Figure 4.1: Illustrations of the proposed TetraSpar concept [7].
Stiesdal’s proposed main idea of the concept is to keep industrialisation in mind, and
hence to utilise technology already known from the wind industry in regards to e.g.
wind turbine manufacturing. For instance, the tapered center column is well known
from wind turbine tower manufacturing. Spiral-welded cylindrical braces are classical
industry technology and structural components often seen on a variety of structures in
general. The buoyancy tanks located at each corner can be made of e.g. steel or glass-
reinforced-plastic and are also classical industry components. Lastly, cast nodes at the
intersection of the braces at the corners, or at the lower end of the central tapered column,
are already known from wind turbine hub manufacturing. Hence all components can
be mass-produced away from port of assembly, facilitating industrialised manufacturing,
while at the same time keeping the weight of the ﬂoater down to save cost. Using a
relatively inexpensive land based crawler crane, instead of a ﬂoating crane vessel, to
assemble the ﬂoater and to mount the wind turbine, the entire ﬂoating wind turbine can
be assembled at quayside in potentially weeks instead of months. Once assembled, the
unit may simply be towed out to site with the ﬂoater having semisub stability. During this
tow from assembly site to installation site, the suspended keel is air-ﬁlled with compressed
air, and thus ﬂoats along with the rest of the ﬂoater. Once on site, the compressed air is
==PAGE:103==
4.1. The TetraSpar Floating Foundation Concept
79
let out of the keel, which subsequently sinks and pull the ﬂoater down, thus making the
ﬂoater act as a spar [7].
Compared to e.g. the TripleSpar which weights in at roughly 30,000 tonnes c.f. Table
2.3, the goal with the TetraSpar is to produce a modular ﬂoater weighting around 1,000-
1,500 tonnes without ballast for a 6 MW turbine. A TetraSpar ﬂoater with dimensions
as proposed by Stiesdal is shown on Figure 4.2. All braces are 2.5 m in diameter and
has a wall thickness of 25 mm. The material thickness of the buoyancy tanks is 12 mm.
The tapered center column has a 50 mm wall thickness at the top and 30 mm at the
bottom. These material thickness’ will not be changed in this study. Depending on the
conﬁguration, the total weight of the ﬂoater without ballast is around 1,100 tonnes. Note
that any discrepancies between the top diameter of the tapered center column and the
wind turbine tower base are neglected for simplicity.

Chapter 4. TetraSpar Model Adaptation, System Identiﬁcation and Optimal Design
4.2
QuLAF Model Adaptation
In this section a description of the most relevant model changes required to simulate the
TetraSpar is provided.
Note that contrary to the TripleSpar model, the TetraSpar model has not been tuned
against a higher ﬁdelity model.
When investigating new design concepts, especially
at such early conceptual stages as in the present works, a higher ﬁdelity time-domain
numerical model might not be available due to their often very long computational time,
while physical model tests most likely is not available either. Recall that the aim with
the present frequency-domain model is to narrow down the design space, before more
comprehensive time-domain simulations are conducted, in order to validate the design
and to provide structural assessments etc.
4.2.1
Floater Mass, Inertia and Draught
Due to the obvious structural diﬀerences compared to the TripleSpar ﬂoater, a new func-
tion for computing the mass moment of inertia of the TetraSpar, as well as related prop-
erties such as the total ﬂoater mass, CoG, draught etc., has been written in Matlab.
These calculations may be consulted in Appendix F for the baseline semisub ﬂoater pro-
posed by Stiesdal. For the TripleSpar the displaced volume was trivially computed using
Archimedes’ principle. This principle will naturally also be utilised here, but as observed
on Figure 4.1 and 4.2, it is evident that the displaced volume of the TetraSpar ﬂoater
signiﬁcantly changes when the draught changes. This was not the case for the TripleSpar
which is basically uniform in the vertical direction. Consequently, the design-speciﬁc
displaced volume of a given TetraSpar design will be precomputed in a look-up table,
for draught values between 0 and 60 m (100 m for the spar conﬁguration). This way
the actual displaced volume will still be determined from Archimedes’ principle. The
volume is then run through the look-up table until a match is found, thus extracting
the corresponding value of ﬂoater draught. Because of the much lighter ﬂoater mass
of the TetraSpar compared to the TripleSpar, the wind turbine has now been included
when computing the draught (the ﬂoater without ballast and the turbine weigh almost
the same). On Figure 4.3 an example of a design-speciﬁc precomputed look-up table of
displaced volume and corresponding draught values may be seen. Lastly, the TetraSpar
also utilises a catenary mooring system with 3 lines in the same conﬁguration as for the
TripleSpar shown on Figure 2.4, hence no signiﬁcant changes were needed in this regard.
4.2.2
WAMIT Computations and Hydrodynamic Forcing
In order to facilitate easy implementation into QuLAF, a simpliﬁed representation of the
TetraSpar was established. Initially, concerning the WAMIT simulations, a simpliﬁed
representation is particularly useful, since the many slender braces of the TetraSpar can
be challenging to model accurately. By merging the three buoyancy tanks located at each
corner of the TetraSpar into one larger tank, the ﬂoater can be said to some extent to
==PAGE:105==
4.2. QuLAF Model Adaptation
81
0
10
20
30
40
50
60
Draught [m]
0
1000
2000
3000
4000
5000
6000
7000
Displaced volume [m3]
Figure 4.3: Design-speciﬁc example of a precomputed look-up table for the TetraSpar ﬂoater
displaced volume and draught. Dashed lines indicate locations for signiﬁcant structural changes.
resemble the TripleSpar, in the sense that it is now composed of three tanks in total. In
relation to the many braces of the TetraSpar, it was decided to distribute the displaced
volume which the braces constitute, into the three large tanks as well. Finally, the center
column was left as it was, since this could already be modelled as a simple cylinder.
Consequently, by ensuring that the total displaced volume of the ﬂoater was matched
in WAMIT, it was possible to greatly simplify these computations, while still capturing
the most important characteristics i.e. diﬀraction and radiation eﬀects. As a ﬁnal note
it should be mentioned that unless the length of the tanks is altered, it is the equivalent
diameter of these three larger tanks that change when matching the displaced volume of
a given design. Also note that the displaced volume is initially determined using nine
smaller tanks, which is than transformed to three larger equivalent tanks.
Having a script that auto-generates the equivalent diameter of the three tanks accord-
ing to the displaced volume, while the diameter of the center column is ﬁxed, entails that
only minor changes to the Morison viscous drag forcing computation is needed. From
the TripleSpar investigations the horizontal and vertical wave kinematics were evaluated
at each of the three columns. The same was the case for the drag force and moment.
Hence it was necessary to include the center column in these calculations based on the
above-mentioned simpliﬁed representation.
This was implemented by considering a
spatial variation when determining the wave kinematics. Since the present model of the
TetraSpar was not tuned against a higher ﬁdelity model, the drag coeﬃcients in surge and
heave where approximated as CD,x = 0.6 and CD,z = 2.0 [20] for all designs considered.
==PAGE:106==
82
Chapter 4. TetraSpar Model Adaptation, System Identiﬁcation and Optimal Design
4.2.3
Cost Function Implementation
In order to determine the most economically feasible design, a cost function was imple-
mented. Co-supervisor Henrik Stiesdal provided costs for the steel (ﬂoater), the iron ore
(ballast material) and the steel mooring chains. Initially, a cost of 1.3 Euro/kg for steel
was considered, but including everything, e.g. surface treatment and handling etc., a
value of 1.5 Euro/kg was used. Iron ore was taken at 50 $/tonne, multiplying this value
by two in order to take into account shipping and potential unforeseen price-ﬂuctuations,
while converting to Euro yields 90 Euro/ton. Finally, the steel mooring chains were taken
as 1.5 $/kg, which when converted to Euro and rounding up is 1.34 Euro/kg. The costs
are further listed in Table 4.1. A quick search on the global trade portal www.alibaba.com
justiﬁes the application of the iron ore cost, while the mooring chain cost might be a lower
than average value. Note that due to the highly industrialised manufacturing of the steel
structural components for the TetraSpar, a rather low cost of steel can be used [7].
When considering the ballast mass no a priori assumptions about ballast geometry
is made. Hence, the suspended ballast is modelled as a point mass in relation to the
inertia calculations. Further, since its geometry is not known, the eﬀective ballast mass will
be considered in the present work when investigating the ballast mass in general. This
means however that when coupling the model to a cost function, the gross ballast mass
must be determined, and not the eﬀective or net ballast mass. The gross volume of the
ballast, Vbal, can be found through
Vbal =
mbal
ρironore −ρw
(4.1)
where mbal is the net or eﬀective ballast mass and ρironore is the density of iron ore
here taken as ρironore = 2600 kg/m3. The cost of a particular design in Euro, C, is then
determined from
C = (mf −mbal)Csteel + (ρironoreVbal)Cironore + 3lmchainCchain + 3zbalmchainCchain
(4.2)
in which mf is the mass of the ﬂoater, l is the unstretched mooring line length, mchain is
the gross mass of mooring chain per m, zbal is the depth of the suspended ballast below
the bottom of the buoyancy tanks and C[′material′] are the respective costs listed in Table
4.1. Note that here both the mooring lines and the chains holding the ballast consist of
the same type of chain.
Table 4.1: Overview of material costs used in the present cost function.
Material costs
Material
Steel
Iron ore
Steel chain

4.3. TetraSpar Suspended Keel System Identiﬁcation
83
4.3
TetraSpar Suspended Keel System Identiﬁcation
In order to initially investigate the aspects of an external suspended keel, a system identi-
ﬁcation on the keel has been conducted. This has been carried out by considering both a
semisub and spar conﬁguration of the TetraSpar. For each conﬁguration a baseline ﬂoater
is designed based on preliminary design estimations from Stiesdal. Note that while these
designs might not be optimal, they are suﬃcient in order to investigate the eﬀects of the
suspended ballast on the system response. In these analyses the eﬀective ballast mass is
varied in increments relative to the amount in the respective baseline ﬂoater. The same
goes for the ballast depth. Note that while in order to achieve stable baseline ﬂoaters,
i.e. compliant with constraints in Table 3.3 subjected to static wind and regular wave
forcing as listed in Table 3.1, it was necessary to extend the water depth from 180 m to 250
m, in order to allow for a lower suspended keel. It is hence already evident that design
optimisation is required subsequently. Note that for the present analyses no focused wave
was considered, as its response characteristics are similar, though larger, compared to the
regular wave. The baseline ﬂoaters are designed such that they yield a hub height of 119
m, thus when varying the ballast mass, the hub height will be altered as well. While this
is suﬃcient at present, later proposed designs will naturally yield a correct hub height of
119 m. For both the semisub and spar conﬁguration, natural frequencies below 0.04 Hz
is desired, with the tower an exception.
4.3.1
TetraSpar Semisub Conﬁguration
A baseline semisub conﬁguration was designed based on preliminary design sketches
provided by Stiesdal, with relevant parameters listed in Table 4.2. A sketch of the semisub
conﬁguration with relevant levels indicated, as well as a ﬁgure of the WAMIT mesh as
previously described, are shown on Figure 4.4. A few things to note; the semisub does not
pierce the waterline in its baseline conﬁguration, however when modifying the ballast
mass a surface piercing ﬂoater will indeed be investigated. Additionally, the WAMIT
mesh utilised is of the same reﬁnement as for the TripleSpar computations (panel size
1 m), here also note the addition of the center column in the computations. Further
note that the mesh shown is for a ﬂoater piercing the waterline; when the ﬂoater is fully
submerged the tanks will be enclosed entirely by panels. Additional details on this will
be given for the spar conﬁguration. In total nine WAMIT simulations (one for each ballast
depth) has been computed.
When varying the ballast mass and depth, the values from Table 4.2 served as a
starting point. On Figure 4.5 the natural frequencies for the semisub conﬁguration are
observed, when varying the ballast mass and ballast depth. Also, the cost function is in-
cluded as well. For visualisation purposes the hypothetical location in the design space,
in relation to an optimal value versus cost is indicated. It is seen that the surge natu-
ral frequency is decreasing when increasing the ballast mass, while keeping the ballast
depth constant. This is as expected since the increased mass will tend to lower the natural

frequency. Keeping the ballast mass constant and increasing the ballast depth, lower
frequencies are also observed. Increasing the ballast depth lowers to CoG. Consequently,
due to the lower CoG, the surge-pitch coupling, i.e. m1,3, will increase in magnitude,
leading to the lower frequencies.
The heave DoF is completely decoupled from the rest of the system, hence a diﬀerence
is only observed when altering the ballast mass. Going from 0 % to -3 % ballast mass,
the ﬂoater will now pierce the waterline, thus a remarkable increase in heave hydrostatic
stiﬀness occurs c.f. eq. (3.12), since the waterplane area of the ﬂoater is now suddenly
==PAGE:109==
4.3. TetraSpar Suspended Keel System Identiﬁcation
85
increased signiﬁcantly. Increasing the ballast mass lowers the heave natural frequency,
which is not directly observable due to the diﬀerence in levels for the present contour
lines.
The pitch natural frequency is a bit harder to deduce. Beginning with the lower half
of the ﬁgure, i.e. for ballast mass in the interval -3 % to -15 % where the buoyancy tanks
pierce the waterline, and the pitch hydrostatic stiﬀness is largest c.f. eq. (3.10). Increas-
ing the ballast depth for constant ballast mass entails a lower natural frequency. This is
expected since the pitch inertia (mass contribution in pitch) must c.f. Figure 4.6) increase
simultaneously, due to a lower CoG. Note that the pitch stiﬀness is also increasing, owing
to the ﬂoater CoG contribution to the pitch stiﬀness as dictated by eq. (3.10). However,
the increase in inertia is more pronounced than the increase in stiﬀness in this case, since
a decrease is observed. Keeping the ballast depth constant and lowering the ballast mass
from -3 % to -15 %, will also yield lower natural frequencies. C.f. Figure 4.6, it is observed
that the inertia and the pitch stiﬀness will decrease when removing ballast mass, naturally
due to the now lower mass and subsequent higher CoG, respectively. Further, as depicted
on Figure G.6 in Appendix G the pitch added mass is lowering for decreasing ballast mass.
The lower inertia and added mass will tend to increase the natural frequency, but since
the frequency is observed to decrease slightly, the decrease in pitch stiﬀness is evidently
more pronounced in this case. The relative change in inertia and added mass is almost
the same as the relative change in stiﬀness however since the frequency changes very
little.
Now investigating the pitch +0 % to +15 % ballast mass interval where the buoyancy
tanks are fully submerged. Keeping the ballast mass constant and increasing the ballast
depth yields lower frequencies, just as when the ﬂoater is only partially submerged. On
Figure 4.6 it is seen that the pitch stiﬀness and pitch inertia is varying in a similar manner
as before, albeit with diﬀerent magnitudes naturally. When on the other hand increasing
the ballast mass for constant depth, a slight increase in natural frequency is observed.
More ballast mass tends to induce larger hydrostatic pitch stiﬀness due to the larger mass,
as well as lower CoG see eq. (3.10), while the displaced volume is not altered much since
the ﬂoater is already fully submerged. This increased stiﬀness will naturally drive the
natural frequency up. Meanwhile however is an increase in pitch inertia again due to the
larger mass and lower CoG, which will tend to lower the natural frequency. Investigating
Figure G.3 a drastic change in the pattern for the pitch added mass is observed, as the
ﬂoater becomes ever more submerged. This makes the pitch natural frequency hard to
predict and deduce, and may also play a role in creating the sweeping rather than straight
contour lines. Given, this is a balance between pitch stiﬀness and total inertia in pitch.
Likely, the change in pitch stiﬀness is more pronounced than the change in inertia.
The tower natural frequency is decreasing with increasing ballast depth and ballast
mass, owing to coupling eﬀects.
On Figure 4.6 the response due to the static wind forcing may be observed. Gen-
erally, increasing the ballast mass will lead to larger surge responses, because the ﬂoater

ballast conﬁgurations indicated by the red line. A possible design should obviously stay
below this constraint.

surge response in the ballast mass interval -3 % to -15 % initially, reveals that for constant
ballast depth and decreasing ballast mass, the response decreases. On Figure G.5 it is seen
that the surge RAO peak actually shifts very slightly towards the wave forcing frequency,
==PAGE:112==
88
Chapter 4. TetraSpar Model Adaptation, System Identiﬁcation and Optimal Design
hence an increase may contrary initially be expected. But owing to less mass more of the
ﬂoater is now pushed up and out of the water, and the cumulative wave forcing on the
ﬂoater will decrease, simply because less area is now seen by the water particles. Despite
the shifting RAO peak, this is the reason for the observed decrease in surge response.
Keeping the ballast mass constant and increasing the ballast depth, a slight increase in
response is observed. On Figure G.4 the surge RAO is moving slightly farther away from
the wave forcing frequency hence a decrease may be expected. However, due to the
strong coupling between surge and pitch, which for increased ballast depth makes the
system more inert, an increase is observed since the coupling m1,3 increases in magni-
tude, again due to lower CoG. This tendency may also be observed on Figure 4.6 for low
ballast mass in the surge response. Investigating the ballast mass interval +0 % to +15 %
a decrease in surge response is seen when increasing the ballast mass for constant depth.
On Figure G.2 the surge RAO peaks shifts to lower frequencies potentially inducing lower
responses. However, the majority of the contribution to the lower response is likely due
to the increased mass of the ﬂoater, coupled with the fact that as more ballast is added,
the ﬂoater sinks deeper reducing the amount of cumulative wave forcing, which is par-
ticularly pronounced around the waterline, and decreasing logarithmically. Keeping the
ballast mass constant and increasing the ballast depth entails larger surge responses. On
Figure G.1 it can be seen that the surge RAO peak shifts very little, hence the reason for
the increasing surge response must be found in the surge-pitch coupling.
The heave response is seen to be insensitive to the ballast depth as expected, owing
to the decoupling. The largest responses occur when the ﬂoater pierces the waterline.
Removing ballast obviously makes the ﬂoater lighter and more susceptible to wave mo-
tions. Further, when removing ballast the bottom of the ﬂoater lies higher in the water,
yielding larger vertical particle velocities which in turn increases the response, which is
observed.
Pitch responses are largest when the ﬂoater pierces the waterline. Investigating the
ballast mass interval -3 % to -15 % for constant ballast depth yields lower responses, as
more ballast is removed which might seem counter-intuitive. As previously discussed,
the natural frequency drops when removing ballast mass, which may also be partially
seen on Figure G.5 where a slight decrease in pitch RAO peak is observed, thus entail-
ing potentially lower responses. Further, as the structure is pushed out of the water
the cumulative wave forcing on the structure drops as well, while the pitch mooring
stiﬀness increases. Keeping the ballast mass constant and increasing the ballast depth,
lower responses are observed, which is more intuitive than the case before. Naturally, the
stiﬀness and inertia is increasing, but as shown on Figure G.4 the pitch RAO noticeably
shifts away from the wave forcing frequency for increasing ballast depth, hence the main
reason for the lower response. Investigating the interval +0 % to +15 % ballast mass the
pitch is somewhat harder to deduce due to the broad contour lines. Generally, the pitch
response is much less when the ﬂoater is fully submerged, and the diﬀerences when vary-
ing the ballast mass and depth are not as pronounced as when the ﬂoater is only partially

submerged. Increasing the ballast mass for constant ballast depth shows the true, and
often not very transparent, dynamic nature of the system. There might be a tendency
for the response to increase slightly and then to decrease again subsequently for large
enough ballast mass values, as indicated by the plateau encircled by the 0.6 deg contour
line. The hydrodynamic properties on Figure G.3 are observed to change remarkably. For
instance, due to the complete submergence, radiation damping is signiﬁcantly less now
due to the diminishing outwards propagating surface waves stemming from the struc-
ture motions. Increasing the ballast depth for constant ballast mass results in a similar
response pattern. Further investigations reveal that going from 120 m to 184 m yields
==PAGE:114==
90
Chapter 4. TetraSpar Model Adaptation, System Identiﬁcation and Optimal Design
slightly larger responses, hereafter it decreases again, thus indicating that several factors
play an important part in the dynamic response, and that it might not be that simple to
deduce at all times. Thus in such cases it is vital to have conﬁdence in the static response
patterns in order to trust the model for dynamic computations.
The nacelle acceleration is inherently linked to the surge and pitch acceleration, hence
the motion response must be a combination of these responses, plus the tower ﬂexibility
contribution. It is observed that the largest nacelle accelerations occur when the ﬂoater is
piercing the waterline, as the surge and pitch responses are also largest here. Investigating
the -3 % to -15 % ballast mass interval for constant ballast depth, a decrease in nacelle
acceleration is seen for lower ballast mass. Because the surge response and pitch response
both decrease with decreasing ballast mass, due to previously mentioned causes, their
accelerations must also decrease. Keeping the ballast mass constant and varying the
ballast depth, decreasing nacelle accelerations are again observed. Despite the fact that
the surge response increases, the pitch response decreases, and is in this conﬁguration
seen to govern the resulting nacelle acceleration. In the interval +0 % to +15 % ballast
mass, it is observed that for constant ballast depth, decreasing nacelle accelerations occur
when increasing the ballast mass. While keeping the ballast mass constant and varying
the ballast depth yields slightly higher accelerations. The cause for this naturally being
that outlined earlier on.
4.3.2
TetraSpar Spar Conﬁguration
The only structural diﬀerences between the baseline semisub conﬁguration and the spar
conﬁguration, is the addition of a 25 m steel transition piece in the latter, with a wall
thickness of 50 mm. The transition piece has been mounted on top of the tapered center
column, in between it and the tower base, in order to increase the ﬂoater submergence
while retaining the 119 m hub height. Relevant parameters are listed in Table 4.3. A
sketch of the spar with relevant levels is shown on Figure 4.8. Also shown is a WAMIT
mesh example for the submerged ﬂoater. Note the addition of a top circular mesh on
the buoyancy tanks, and the larger length of center column due to the increased submer-
gence. It is noted that only the transition piece pierces the waterline in this conﬁguration.
Again, nine WAMIT simulations have been conducted for each ballast mass. The inertia
calculations have naturally been modiﬁed as well, in order to take into account the ad-
dition of a transition piece. These calculations have been omitted here for brevity. As
before the ballast mass and depth is varied around the values listed in Table 4.3, note the
initially shorter ballast depth compared to the semisub.
On Figure 4.9 the natural frequencies for the spar conﬁguration of the TetraSpar
concept are shown, for varying ballast mass and ballast depth. Increasing the ballast
mass is naturally seen to yield lower surge natural frequencies. In addition, the surge
mooring stiﬀness will reduce as the fairlead height on the ﬂoater reduces. On Figure
G.9 in Appendix G it can additionally be seen that the surge added mass also increases
contributing to the lower natural frequencies. Increasing the ballast depth for constant

ballast mass also results in lower frequencies. This is due to the coupling between surge
and pitch, as the CoG is altered.
The completely decoupled heave natural frequency is not altered with the depth of
the ballast, but only slightly with the ballast mass. This indicates a balance between the
decreasing heave mooring stiﬀness and increasing ballast mass.
Concerning pitch; increasing the ballast mass for constant depth increases the fre-
quency. As seen on Figure 4.10 the total pitch stiﬀness increases. Dictated by eq. 3.10
the pitch hydrostatic stiﬀness must increase for additional ballast mass, since the mass
increases and the CoG gets lower, while displaced volume is not altered signiﬁcantly.
However, the pitch inertia also increases and so does the added mass, as seen on Figure
4.10 and G.9, respectively. Increasing the ballast depth for constant ballast mass results
in a peculiar pattern. On Figure 4.10 it can be seen that the spacing between the pitch
stiﬀness contour lines are more uniform than the spacing for the pitch inertia, which for
suﬃciently large ballast depths, will have a larger incremental increase. As a result the
pitch natural frequency contour lines sweep the way they do, once the increase in inertia
becomes more pronounced than the increase in stiﬀness.
Again, the tower natural frequency is decreasing with increasing ballast depth and
ballast mass, owing to coupling eﬀects.
On Figure 4.10 the response to the static wind forcing is seen. Increasing the ballast
mass yields larger surge responses, due to the lower fairlead positions and subsequent
lower mooring stiﬀness. Increasing the depth entails larger pitch inertia and owing to

the surge-pitch coupling, the surge response decreases slightly. The same can be slightly
observed for the semisub conﬁguration around 15 % additional ballast mass on Figure
4.6.
Considering pitch response it is as expected seen that increasing both the ballast
mass and ballast depth yields lower pitch responses. This is because of the increasing
stiﬀness. Naturally, the static pitch response is derived solely from the forcing and stiﬀ-
ness. Also observable is the pitch operational constraint indicated by the red contour
line. The response due to regular wave forcing is seen on Figure 4.11. Increasing the
ballast mass a decrease in surge response is observed. First and foremost, because of
the resulting increase in draught, the wave kinematics and resulting forces acting will be
lower resulting in decreasing response. This despite the concurrent decrease in mooring
stiﬀness, which may not come into play if the ﬂoater is not displaced suﬃciently from
its equilibrium position. Further, as depicted on Figure G.9 the surge added mass and
damping is increasing, while the wave excitation forces are decreasing, also resulting in
lower responses. Lastly, observing the surge RAO peak on Figure G.8 a shift away from
the wave forcing frequency is observed which also results in potentially lower responses.
Keeping the ballast mass constant and increasing the ballast depth, larger responses are
observed. The RAO peak on Figure G.7 does not change signiﬁcantly. Consequently, this
can only be explained by the surge-pitch coupling which increases in magnitude due to
the lower CoG.
Considering heave, it is evident that only the ballast mass aﬀects the response due
to the decoupling. Increased ballast mass increases the draught and consequently the
vertical wave forces become smaller. On Figure G.9 it is clear the also the wave excitation
forces decrease.
