4.4
New Site-dependant TetraSpar Design
Understanding the main physical phenomena behind the external suspended ballasted
keel, as well as having tested the implementation of the cost function, a new site-
dependant conﬁguration of the TetraSpar ﬂoater will be proposed in this section.
So far, it can be deduced that especially the semisub in its above conﬁguration may be
very susceptible to wave forcing due to its in general inconvenient heave and pitch nat-
ural frequencies. The spar on the other hand, though in the above-shown conﬁguration
requiring a bit more steel material, has far better natural frequency characteristics and
may turn out to be a more suitable conﬁguration.
==PAGE:120==
96
Chapter 4. TetraSpar Model Adaptation, System Identiﬁcation and Optimal Design
In order to conﬁne the design space for the present design optimisation, only a few
parameters were chosen to be parametrised. C.f. Table 3.2, the mooring system was
excluded from the parametrisation, due to its redundancy in modifying heave properties.
In keeping with the TetraSpar concept idea, dimensions of structural components ought
to be chosen wisely in order to facilitate industrialised manufacturing and ease of trans-
port and assembly. Hence, too long buoyancy tank lengths should be avoided. While
larger tanks might be possible to manufacture, they are potentially diﬃcult to handle
with a crane at quayside if too long, and may require additional internal stiﬀening or
a thicker wall thickness, in order to ensure structural reliability. This leaves the ballast
mass, buoyancy tank diameter and ﬂoater radius as decision variables to be parametrised.
Keeping all three parameters would result in n3 computations with the method intended
here. Trying to reduce this amount of computations, it can be argued that the ballast
mass might be the most insigniﬁcant parameter of these three, in relation to cost-driving
factors. This is because the ballast may be delivered in bulk quantities, whereas structural
components naturally need to be manufactured which is more labour intensive and thus
a larger cost-driving factor. Consequently, it was decided to vary the buoyancy tank
diameter and ﬂoater radius only, while modifying the ballast mass in order to achieve the
correct hub height of the wind turbine, ultimately resulting in n2 computations.
4.4.1
Environmental Conditions and Procedure
While the static wind forcing, i.e.
2Frated, is still a good starting point in relation to
determining the minimum pitch stiﬀness required, the regular wave forcing basically
serves no practical purpose when designing for real site conditions. Thus revised and
more true-to-life environmental conditions are needed. In the present ad hoc design
process when investigating the dynamic response of proposed designs, four design load
cases are considered which are
1. ﬂuctuating wind at rated wind speed with an irregular sea state
2. ﬂuctuating wind at rated wind speed without waves
3. ﬂuctuating wind at maximum operational wind speed without waves
4. single focused wave without wind
Note that the static wind forcing using 2Frated is still utilised initially before conducting
dynamic simulations, in order to exclude possible designs not having suﬃcient pitch
stiﬀness.
The ﬂuctuating wind at the turbine’s rated wind speed and the corresponding thrust
has been precomputed in FAST for augmented exactness. The thrust has not been pre-
computed for a wind speed of 25 m/s, i.e. maximum speed before cut-out c.f. Table
2.2. As a consequence of this, it will be computed using eq. (2.56), with the ﬂuctuating
wind determined by a Kaimal wind spectrum. Due to the high wind speed of 25 m/s,
==PAGE:121==
4.4. New Site-dependant TetraSpar Design
97
a turbulence intensity of 20 % has been assumed for that case. Further, note that also
aerodynamic damping has been precomputed in FAST. For the 25 m/s wind speed the
damping has been extrapolated which may limit exactness of this parameter.
The irregular sea state employed in this study is the 50th year sea state (i.e. 50 year
return period) taken from the Hywind Buchan Deep Metocean Design Basis [28]. It is
characterised by a JONSWAP spectrum with a signiﬁcant wave height of Hs = 10.5 m
and a peak wave period of Tp = 14.3 s. Finally, the focused wave is not much diﬀerent
from that shown in Figure 3.2. However, the wave energy is now computed using the
above-outlined values for Hs and Tp, while the crest elevation is determined using [20]
Hmax = 1.86Hs
(4.3)
In this sense Hmax is in the present study utilised to deﬁne the maximum surface elevation
of the focused wave (the single crest height), which becomes α = 19.53 m, and thus not
the actual wave height. An overview of the applied forcing with relevant parameters is
provided in Table 4.4. The mean thrust for the two wind load cases are also shown; note
the lower thrust at 25 m/s which is due to the blade pitch-to-feather mechanism (the
amount of power generated for this wind speed is however the same as at rated wind
speed).

the time series amount to roughly one hour and ﬁfteen minutes in total. Considering
that [26] recommends running a design sea state for a duration of 3-6 hours, it has been
chosen to compute a total of six realisations/simulations of the available 1 hour time
series, including the irregular wave forcing. The actual response is then taken as the
mean of the six 6th largest responses, which are computed for each realisation. This was
done in order not to drastically oversize the design for the 50th year sea state, compared
to using a total duration of six hours. The six realisations have each been computed with a
diﬀerent seed number in order to yield a diﬀerent phase of each individual wind (Kaimal)
and wave component. The seed number sequence is retained for all realisations for the
various designs investigated, in order to compare the performance of the designs to one
another. The seed numbers have been chosen by requesting a random number between
1 and 100 six times in Matlab, and are listed in Table 4.6.

The actual design procedure was carried out as follows. Based on the two chosen design
parameters
• buoyancy tank diameter
• ﬂoater radius
it was decided to vary these within certain bounds to conﬁne the design space within
reasonable limits. The buoyancy tank diameter was varied in the interval [4 8] m in
increments of 0.5 m, while the ﬂoater radius was varied in the interval [33 49] m in
increments of 2 m, yielding a total of 89 simulations for each ﬂoater conﬁguration. Recall
the ballast mass was for each design altered to yield the correct hub height of the turbine.
Initially, simulations were run in order to investigate the static wind pitch response,
with concurrently varying the ballast depth such that the operational pitch constraint
listed in Table 3.3 was complied with. It is also possible to alter the mooring lines to
tune the static response in both surge, heave and pitch, as well as to modify the natural
frequencies. In the ﬁrst iteration it was decided to assume an angle between the mooring
line and fairlead of 45 deg, thus rewriting eq. (2.58) to

(4.8)
where the unknown total line force T is solved for and φw is the angle between line and
fairlead which is predetermined. By adjusting X (indirectly the anchor radius) it was
possible to alter the mooring line length and thus the restoring stiﬀness, while keeping
the mass of the line constant for a ﬁxed fairlead angle. After several computations it
was however decided that a bit more control over the mooring system would be desir-
able, since rather long (and expensive) mooring lines were needed for the above-outlined
method to yield reasonable mooring stiﬀness’. Based on this initial static analysis which
was coupled to the cost function, the most cost-eﬃcient designs could be located within
==PAGE:125==
4.4. New Site-dependant TetraSpar Design
101
the design space, along with other potentially interesting designs. The chosen designs
then underwent a dynamic analysis in which the forcing listed in Table 4.4 was used in
the four design load cases outlined in the beginning of this subsection. Subsequently, an
evaluation of the designs may be conducted, and the process may be either repeated with
new designs, or terminated if reasonable and compliant designs were found. Naturally,
the designs have to comply with the operational constrains listed in Table 3.3. When
subjected to a focused wave however, it was deemed suﬃcient to comply with the non-
operational constraints. Lastly, during this design process, natural frequencies below 0.04
Hz were considered especially desirable due to the resulting mitigation of wave excita-
tion.
Since irregular waves are random in nature, comparing time series of responses does
not serve a practical purpose. Instead, exceedance probability plots are utilised as es-
tablished in [30], enabling a more appropriate comparison between the responses of the
various designs. The exceedance probability, P , is deﬁned as
P = 1 −
i
N + 1
(4.9)
in which i refers to an index and N is the total number of peaks. Brieﬂy, the basic idea is
to sort each peak, determined by a zero-crossing analysis, in the time series and sort them
from minimum to maximum, giving them an index i. In this sense the largest peak will
correspond to the lowest probability. The probability plots are shown in semi-logarithmic
plots for augmented visualisation purposes.
4.4.2
New Semisub Conﬁguration
Initially, a new semisub conﬁguration was investigated. A redesign of the previously
investigated design shown on Figure 4.4 was needed, since this baseline semisub design
did not pierce the waterline as it ought to in this conﬁguration. As listed in Table 4.5 the
new semisub must have a freeboard of at least 12 m, in order to mitigate the eﬀects of green
water loading on top of the tanks. Design considerations in relation to concurrent heave
motions were not considered in regards to freeboard, only in relation to the depth of the
ballast in order to ensure that it would not rest on the sea bed; a maximum heave response
of 8.76 m was assumed for the semisub and later for the spar conﬁguration also. In order
to ensure suﬃcient freeboard the buoyancy tanks were extended and ﬁxed at an elevation
of 12 m above the waterline for all computations, with the fairleads attached at the top.
Further, a possibility to extend the length of the buoyancy tanks downwards measured
from the horizontal braces is incorporated. From the prior analysis on the semisub, it
became clear that its natural frequencies were very undesirable. C.f. Figure 3.13 it should
be possible to lower the natural frequencies by increasing the length of the buoyancy
tanks.
This was done despite the tanks should not be too long in order to facilitate
relatively easy handling. On Figure 4.14 a sketch of the new semisub conﬁguration is
shown. Note the variable tank length measured from the level of tank intersection with
==PAGE:126==
102
Chapter 4. TetraSpar Model Adaptation, System Identiﬁcation and Optimal Design
the horizontal braces. In [28] it is stated that the water depth at Buchan Deep is assumed
within the interval 100-140 m. A water depth of 120 m for the semisub conﬁguration is
used here. Further, when varying the ballast depth and adjusting for static pitch response,
it is made sure that the ballast is not actually resting on the sea bed, potentially inducing
slack ballast chains which would be critical to the ﬂoater stability.

Figure 4.14: New semisub conﬁguration with variable buoyancy tank length.
Further, a few details concerning the mooring system were altered. While still consisting
of three catenary mooring lines, and besides the relocation of fairleads to the top of the
tanks, the gross mooring line mass was reduced to 220 kg/m, while the unstretched line
length was set to 690 m, with an anchor radius of 707.5 m used for the ﬂoater with a
radius of 35 m and tank diameter of 5 m, which resembles the baseline semisub ﬂoater.
This mooring conﬁguration provided reduced stiﬀness (and frequency) due to the lower
mass and relatively long length, while still providing reasonable static pitch response, in
conjunction with the ballast depth alteration. The mooring system was not further al-
tered during the present computations, since the eﬀect on the system was not signiﬁcant
compared to e.g. the tank diameter, and that excellent static pitch responses were found.
81 (+6 due to longer tank lengths) WAMIT simulations and QuLAF computations
were conducted resulting in the static results shown on Figure 4.15, using initially a tank
length of 20 m only. The jaggedness of the pitch constraint at 6 deg is due to the tuning
of the ballast depth for designs close to this constraint. For designs yielding suﬃcient
pitch stiﬀness a ballast depth of no less than 50 m was assumed, since despite all these
designs having suﬃcient stiﬀness in relation to static forcing, maybe even without a keel, a
suspended keel at suﬃcient depth was deemed necessary for the dynamic computations.
It is initially noted that the static pitch response seems reasonable for most of the designs
==PAGE:127==
4.4. New Site-dependant TetraSpar Design
103
within the present bounds, with only the very compact designs not yielding suﬃcient
stiﬀness. Naturally, due to the buoyancy tanks piercing the waterline, the hydrostatic
stiﬀness will be high, and static responses consequently low for many of the designs. In
this sense, due to the piercing tanks, it is as previously observed that the heave and pitch
natural frequencies however are above the desired limit of 0.04 Hz, with some even close
to the peak wave frequency of 0.0699 Hz. This may potentially have a severe eﬀect on the
applicability of the present conﬁguration, for the desired site at Buchan Deep.
Three designs were chosen for further dynamic investigation. The two cheapest, but
still inherently diﬀerent designs denoted by 1⃝and 2⃝, and a third representing a more
expensive alternative, with a larger tank diameter. Running design 1⃝, 2⃝and 3⃝through
a dynamic simulation with the load case consisting of wind and wave forcing, it quickly
became apparent that they were all severely aﬀected by the wave forcing owing to the
close proximity between he natural frequencies and peak wave frequency. At this stage
only the load case involving ﬂuctuating wind at rated wind speed and irregular waves
were considered, since the designs must comply with all load cases and that the wave
forcing in this case is the most critical, owing to the low peak frequency. Next, it was
decided to try and modify the tank length in order to adjust the natural frequencies.
Computations were again conducted for design 1⃝, 2⃝and 3⃝, each with a column length
of 5 m, 20 m and 35 with appropriate WAMIT input. The pitch responses and nacelle
accelerations determined are shown on Figure 4.16 and 4.17, with the heave and surge
responses, as well as RAOs for a tank legth of 20m, shown in Appendix H.1. Natural
frequencies and other relevant parameters are further listed in Table 4.7, where it is ob-
served that when increasing the tank length, the general trend is indeed lower natural
frequencies as expected, with an exception being the pitch natural frequency for design
1⃝. This indicates that the results from the sensitivity analysis on the TripleSpar, may only
be guiding when applied to another type of ﬂoater.
It is immediately apparent that design 3⃝, which is actually the most expensive as
well as extensive of the three, is also by far the worst performing. The heave and pitch
natural frequencies of design 3⃝are, despite modifying the tank length, close to the peak
wave frequency of 0.0699 Hz and are as a consequence severely excited by this, yielding
the large responses observed. The pitch responses of 30-35 deg observed on Figure 4.16,
might even cause the righting moment to become smaller than the overturning moment,
resulting in a total loss of stability. Further, the nacelle accelerations close to 2g, shown
on Figure 4.17, are much larger than allowable.
Design 1⃝and 2⃝yield in general mutually similar responses as seen on Figure 4.16
and 4.17. Even though neither comply with the constraints listed in Table 3.3. The main
reason for their somewhat better response characteristics, is that their natural frequencies,
as observed in Table 4.7, are located farther away from the peak wave frequency, resulting
in less wave excitation.
While it was possible to alter the natural frequencies by locating the fairleads at the
bottom of the tanks, and simultaneously altering the mooring lines, this was far from

suﬃcient to bring down the natural frequencies enough, in order to comply even with

==PAGE:129==
4.4. New Site-dependant TetraSpar Design
105
immense responses again owing to the resonance conditions being satisﬁed.
Despite the TripleSpar ﬂoater also piercing the waterline, its mass is much larger than
is the case for the TetraSpar. As a consequence, the natural frequencies are signiﬁcantly
higher for the TetraSpar, which results in the profound responses observed here owing to
wave excitation. In order to design a viable semisub conﬁguration compliant with con-
straints for the present environmental conditions from Buchan Deep, it will be necessary
to look into other design parameters than those investigated here. In keeping with the
TetraSpar concept however, the mass of the actual ﬂoater should be kept at a minimum. It
is the author’s impression that it might not be possible to design a semisub conﬁguration
of the TetraSpar, compliant with the present sea state, thus necessitating the need for a
diﬀerent conﬁguration, e.g. in the form of the spar conﬁguration. The semisub conﬁgu-
ration may however be compliant in less severe sea states, where a higher peak natural
frequency is found, thus potentially mitigating the eﬀects of wave excitation.

4.4.3
New Spar Conﬁguration
As shown earlier, the spar conﬁguration of the TetraSpar concept has superior response
and natural frequency characteristics compared to the semisub. Initially, no structural
changes are performed during the ﬁrst iteration, but the fairlead locations have been
shifted to the bottom of the tanks for increased stability. C.f. Table 4.5 the spar conﬁgu-
ration should have a submergence, measured from the top of the buoyancy tanks to the
waterline, larger than 9 m, in order to ensure the tanks do not pierce the waterline at
any time, which would signiﬁcantly alter the dynamics. Due to its deeper draught, the
deepest water depth at Buchan Deep of 140 m is initially considered for this conﬁguration.
It will later be revealed that a spar conﬁguration is also found compliant with e.g. a 100 m
water depth. Since no vertical structural components are varied in the present analysis,
and that the hub height has to be maintained at 119 m for all designs, the submergence
(and draught) will be the same for all designs. Again, when modifying the ballast depth
to adjust the static pitch response, it is made sure that the ballast is not actually resting on
the sea bed at any given time, in this sense a maximum heave response of 9 m is assumed.
A spar conﬁguration sketch is shown on Figure 4.18.

constraints, with the nacelle acceleration operational constraint being exceeded for design
1⃝when subjected to a focused wave, which here is deemed ﬁne due to the severity of the
forcing, and should as mentioned just stay below the non-operational constraint. From
Table 4.8 it may be observed that design 1⃝and 3⃝have similar responses characteristics
for all load cases, disregarding focused wave forcing. This is interesting since these are
the cheapest and most expensive, respectively. Design 2⃝has for combined wind and
wave forcing better response characteristics than design 1⃝and 3⃝, due to the lower pitch
natural frequency, but is worse when subjected only to wind forcing. In general, design
2⃝and 3⃝which have a large ﬂoater radius perform better when subjected to a focused
wave, and even stay within the operational constraints.
C.f.
Appendix H.2 all designs have maximum surge responses around 20-25 m,
and heave responses ranging from 3-11 m. While it may be possible to lower the pitch
natural frequency by modifying the mooring lines using e.g. lighter chains, the restoring
stiﬀness in the other DoFs will as a result also become lower, potentially allowing for even
larger surge responses and static pitch responses. It is estimated that surge responses
much larger than 20-25 m should be avoided, since this may interfere with other ﬂoaters’
mooring chains or its own land power cable. Further, the mooring system has at present
been linearised around its equilibrium position. Having surge responses in the order of
20 m, lifting and lowering of mooring chains on the sea bed, upwind and downwind
of the turbine respectively, will induce asymmetrical restoring forces, which ought to be
included in the simulations by e.g. linearising the mooring system around the ﬂoater’s
location at rated power. Another possible way to lower the pitch natural frequency would
be to modify some of the structural components, e.g.
by increasing the tank length.
Increasing the amount of steel naturally entails increased cost and as a consequence, the
use of longer tanks, or any other structural component, to lower the natural frequency
should thus be weighted against the advantages. For the present designs for instance,
neither strictly conform to the desired natural frequency limit of 0.04 Hz. Nonetheless,
all still perform within the imposed constraints when subjected to a 50th year sea state.
In the end, it is up to the client to decide what they want; lowering the natural frequency
suﬃciently may turn out to be a costly path, for potentially minimal gains.
In Table 4.9 detailed properties of the three spar designs may be consulted. Due to
their somewhat similar response characteristics, a natural choice would be to suggest the
cheapest ﬂoater, design 1⃝, as a proposed conﬁguration at Buchan Deep, assuming the
water depth is suﬃcient (140 m). Otherwise, design 3⃝would apply which is applicable
from a water depth of 100 m. Compared to design 2⃝and 3⃝, design 1⃝is a more compact
ﬂoater. This has the added beneﬁt of allowing easier handling with cranes at quayside,
and it may also navigate narrower passageways such as e.g. harbour inlets, entailing a
wider applicability in relation to port of assembly. A sketch of design 1⃝is provided on
Figure 4.22.

It is the author’s impression that the TetraSpar type ﬂoating foundation is a viable con-
cept, suited for further research and optimisation. While the present study showed that
the semisub conﬁguration has severe limitations, at least when exposed to severe sea
states, the spar conﬁguration on the other hand showed excellent response characteris-
tics. The main reason that the semisub was not shown to be a viable solution, is due to
the combination of the ﬂoater’s large waterplane area which entails a large hydrostatic
stiﬀness, with the combination of the ﬂoater’s lower mass and pitch inertia. This com-
bination yields relatively high natural frequencies, which meant that for the design sea
state chosen in this study, the heave and pitch motions were excited. It can not be ruled
out that a compliant semisub design may be found for a less severe sea state however.
The natural frequencies of the spar conﬁguration were much lower, due to its smaller
waterplane area. As a result the motions were not excited and several stable designs were
found.
As noted by [9] main drivers for cost savings are the possibility to reduce the structural
mass, develop modular designs suitable for serial fabrication and optimised installation
processes i.e. utilising land-based cranes by virtue of quayside assembly. The TetraSpar
concept is complaint with these requirements. Concurrently while writing this thesis,
physical model tests of the TetraSpar in both a semisub and spar conﬁguration were
carried out at DHI, Denmark. These tests may further validate the concept, and initial
results from these tests showed excellent response characteristics. Despite the promising
initial ﬁndings, further research and design optimisation is recommended. Based on
==PAGE:137==
4.4. New Site-dependant TetraSpar Design
113
this study it is recommended proceeding especially with the spar conﬁguration, where
further design optimisation may result in an even more material-eﬃcient ﬂoater than the
one proposed here. In a future study expanding the parametrisation to take into account
vertical structural components, and the layout and amount of buoyancy tanks, would be
relevant.

==PAGE:138==

Chapter 4. TetraSpar Model Adaptation, System Identiﬁcation and Optimal Design
Due to the present limited amount of operating ﬂoating wind turbines, the costs of future
commercial projects are diﬃcult to assess, since the installed prototypes not necessarily
provide a reasonable cost estimate in this regard. Statoil for instance hopes to cut costs by
two thirds with the commencement of Hywind Scotland ﬂoating wind farm, compared
to the 2009 prototype. Further, trying to predict commercial costs implies assuming e.g.
site conditions, distance to shore, wind farms size etc., which will naturally not be the
same for each wind farm. Despite these limitations, [9] estimates that by the early 2020s,
average CAPEX for ﬂoating wind will be at least 2.9m Euro/MW (2.6m £/MW), which
includes the wind turbine, foundation, moorings and anchors, installation, balancing
of system and decommissioning. [9] further estimates that the foundation alone will
constitute roughly 22.5 % of the CAPEX. This means that the foundation alone will be
around 6.53m Euro for a 10 MW turbine, with the above cost estimate. In the present
study a ﬂoater cost for design 1⃝of 3.73m Euro is found, which is slightly over half that
of the estimate, and even also includes the mooring system. Despite being estimations
only, it might be argued that the TetraSpar concept has the potential to yield signiﬁcant
disruption in ﬂoating wind costs. Lastly, for comparison, in [13] an optimal TripleSpar
design is produced having a CAPEX of 9.4m Euro for the ﬂoater, which is 2.5 times that
of the TetraSpar. Naturally, comparisons are hard to make directly due to the lack of
ﬂoating wind turbines in operation. However, these initial estimates are promising, and
may provide further incentives in relation to continued research on the TetraSpar concept,
thus bringing down the cost of ﬂoating wind and making it commercially attractive.
==PAGE:139==
Chapter5
Conclusions
This thesis investigated parametric design processes for oﬀshore ﬂoating wind turbine
foundations, mounting the DTU 10 MW reference wind turbine. The foundations inves-
tigated in this study were the USTUTT TripleSpar and Stiesdal TetraSpar.
A quick linear frequency-domain numerical model develop in Matlab at DTU Wind
Energy by Antonio M. P. Jurado was used. The model, termed QuLAF, is an aero-hydro-
elastic code, which takes aerodynamic and hydrodynamic loads as well as structural
dynamics into account, in order to simulate the response of a ﬂoating wind turbine. The
model permits fast simulation times by virtue of running simulations in the frequency-
domain, and is thus ideally suited for conceptual design stages and a parametric design
process. The 3+1 DoF model simulates ﬂoater surge, heave and pitch response as well
as tower modal deﬂection. Further, input from the radiation-diﬀraction solver WAMIT
is utilised while viscous eﬀects are modelled through the Morison drag term. Aerody-
namic loads may be either generated internally or applied through precomputed FAST
aerodynamic data. A catenary mooring system linearised around the ﬂoater equilibrium
position is used.
QuLAF which is originally developed in model scale 1/60 for the TripleSpar ﬂoater
supporting the DTU 10 MW RWT, and tuned against the higher ﬁdelity numerical model
FAST, is initially scaled up to full size for application in the present work. A sensitivity
analysis was performed on the upscaled TripleSpar ﬂoater under static wind, regular wave
and focused wave forcing. Here, the ballast mass, column diameter, -spacing, -length,
mooring line length and mooring line mass was varied, in order to explore the design
space. Next, it was possible to investigate how much the parameters could be varied,
with the turbine still conforming to operational and non-operational constraints related
to the pitch response and nacelle acceleration. The design space was further explored by
considering two alternative TripleSpar conﬁgurations, having twice and half a column
spacing respectively, of that of the original TripleSpar. The model was then adapted
to consider the new TetraSpar concept which focuses on industrialisation. Initially, a
system identiﬁcation on the characteristics of an external suspended ballasted keel was
performed, for both a semisub and spar conﬁguration of the TetraSpar. Finally, a design
115
==PAGE:140==
116
Chapter 5. Conclusions
compliant with the 50th year sea state at Buchan Deep, Scotland, was established, by
conducting a parametric investigation in relation to the ﬂoater radius and buoyancy tank
diameter of the TetraSpar. Both a semisub and spar conﬁguration was considered with
both coupled to a cost function, in order to determine the most cost-eﬃcient compliant
design.
The sensitivity analysis established connections between the six input parameters and
their inﬂuence on the system response, when exposed to various forcing. While some of
the results were somewhat intuitive; e.g. larger ballast mass and column spacing would
limit static pitch response, others were not. For instance, the mooring system signiﬁ-
cantly alters characteristics in surge, while they are redundant in heave as modelled in
the present study. This is likely due to the negligence of mooring line mass on the total
system mass however. Consequently, the sensitivity analysis resulted in augmented in-
sight into the chosen input parameters and their inﬂuence on the system.
The constraints or design limits investigation resulted in certain bounds on some of
the input parameters when varied one at a time. The focused wave forcing exceeded
the nacelle acceleration non-operation constraint for all values of the investigated input
parameters, underlining the severity of such a wave. The regular wave yielded responses
that did conform to the operational pitch and nacelle acceleration constraints, for all
values of input parameters. The static wind pitch response was most susceptible to the
variation of input parameters, with the operational constraint exceeded for several values.
Removing e.g. more than 5 % of the original amount of ballast mass, or reducing the
column spacing from 26 m to 23 m would result in a pitch of more than 6 deg. This em-
phasises the importance of assessing static pitch response, i.e. minimum pitch stiﬀness
requirements, before conducting dynamic analyses, since sudden severe gusts of wind
may momentarily result in 2 or 3 times the turbine’s rated thrust and thus potentially
large tilt.
The extended design space exploration and design process concerning the two alter-
native TripleSpar conﬁgurations, while not resulting in practically feasible designs due
to their massive weight, underlined the necessity of having a thorough understanding
of the eﬀects of the chosen input parameters. A ’spiral of increasing dimensions’ was
encountered, where for e.g. to obtain a slightly lower pitch natural frequency, the design
had to be increased far beyond feasible limits. By virtue of the prior conducted sensitivity
analysis, this phenomenon could be identiﬁed, and the design process terminated before
too many resources were wasted pursuing an infeasible design.
The newly proposed TetraSpar concept featuring an external suspended keel is found
to be a generally viable concept, which simultaneously emphasises industrialised man-
ufacturing and overall weight savings. A semisub and spar conﬁguration underwent
parametric optimisation in relation to two input parameters; namely the ﬂoater radius
and buoyancy tank diameter. Using the 50th year sea state at Buchan Deep, it was found
that for the semisub conﬁguration a viable design could not be found. This was due to the
combination of the semisub’s large waterplane area, which yields high hydrostatic stiﬀ-
==PAGE:141==
5.1. Recommendations and Future Work
117
ness’ in heave and pitch, combined with the ﬂoater’s low mass. This consequently entails
high natural frequencies which would make the ﬂoater severely excited by the chosen sea
state. Further, altering the buoyancy tank length was not enough in altering the natural
frequencies suﬃciently away from the peak wave frequency. It was concluded that for
severe or generally large sea states, the semisub conﬁguration is infeasible and potentially
non-compliant. The semisub conﬁguration may however be applicable where calmer sea
states and thus higher peak wave frequencies prevail, or by changing other structural
components than those investigated in this study. The spar conﬁguration showed to be
far more viable than the semisub in the present application, and several diﬀerent designs
for Buchan Deep were proposed, with the cheapest ﬂoater being ultimately suggested,
consisting of a 33 m ﬂoater radius and 7 m buoyancy tank diameter. Due to the spar’s
smaller waterplane area but somewhat similar mass, its natural frequencies were lower
which resulted in less wave excitation compared to the semisub. The natural frequency
in pitch was however above the desired limit of 0.04 Hz. Nonetheless, this did not result
in any limitations on applicability for the present 50th year sea state.
5.1
Recommendations and Future Work
With its excellent response characteristics and industrialised manufacturing, it is sug-
gested to continue research on especially the spar conﬁguration of the TetraSpar concept.
Despite limited cost comparisons, the concept might very well be what is required to
yield disruptive costs within ﬂoating wind.
In order to further improve on the accuracy of the results and on the spar conﬁguration
and TetraSpar concept in general, it is suggested to
• conduct a parametric design process focusing solely on the TetraSpar spar con-
ﬁguration and thus excluding the TripleSpar from considerations. Concurrently
investigating a larger design space for the TetraSpar might result in even more eﬃ-
cient designs and thus help bring down cost. This investigation might also include
the replacing of the steel buoyancy tanks with e.g. GRP tanks which are cheaper,
but also lighter, thus inﬂuencing the dynamics as well.
• tune the QuLAF model against a higher ﬁdelity model in order to validate the
present ﬁndings. Using a higher ﬁdelity model, a pitch controller could be included
as well.
Further, due to the low natural frequencies of the spar conﬁguration,
investigation of the inﬂuence of wave sub-harmonics would also be interesting.
• investigate various model changes diﬀerent from ﬂoater design i.e. linearise the
mooring system around the ﬂoater displaced position at rated thrust, running other
sea states, employ the full size and not shortened wind turbine tower or possibly
even utilising a taut catenary mooring system.

overview.

Ballast in Columns
Within the bottom of each of the hollow columns is located a certain amount of ballast,
in order to stabilize the ﬂoating wind turbine. This ballast will obviously also contribute
to the ﬂoater inertia and hence must be taken into account. Here subscript bal is an
abbreviation for ballast.
Knowing the total mass of the ballast in the system, Mbal, the amount or mass of
ballast located in each column, M1,bal, is
M1,bal = Mbal

Appendix B
WAMIT Convergence Analysis
A convergence analysis when increasing the number of panels was conducted. The con-
vergence analysis was of particular interest since, besides the demand to comply with the
DNV regulation in [26], simulations in WAMIT for many diﬀerent ﬂoater conﬁgurations
were to be carried out, and hence minimizing the computational eﬀort required to run
these simulations would be desirable.
In order to comply with DNV-RP-C205, it was necessary to utilize the direct solver in
WAMIT because the iterative solver did not yield converging results. The time required
to run the direct solver is proportional to (NEQN)3, where NEQN is the number of equa-
tions. When employing the low-order method the number of equations is equal to the
number of panels. The iterative solver on the other hand requires time proportional to
(NEQN)2 signiﬁcantly reducing the computational eﬀort. Obviously, the fastest simula-
tion time is achieved if the iterative solver can be used in conjunction with a relative low
number of panels.
When using a very ﬁne discretised mesh (complying with the DNV regulation) and
at the same time running simulations for very short periods/high frequencies, WAMIT
would crash and terminate as suﬃcient memory could not be allocated to the program
from the WAMIT server. This occurred despite specifying WAMIT to allow usage of up
to 10GB (0.5GB default) of free RAM on the WAMIT server through the Conﬁguration
File. As a consequence of this, the lowest period was gradually increased until a stable
simulation with the reﬁned mesh was achieved, as the very high frequency results were
not deemed as important as the lower frequency ones. The maximum diagonal panel
length associated with the chosen wave period was determined by iterating eq. (2.32)
on a 180 m water depth, and dividing the found wave length with 6 as stated in [26]. A
minimum wave period of 2.475 s yielded a stable simulation, and a maximum diagonal
panel length of Lpanel,max = 1.5935 m was determined based on this period. For a given
mesh, the actual diagonal panel length can be determined from
Lpanel =
q
(x2 −x1)2 + (y2 −y1)2 + (z2 −z1)2
(B.1)
133
==PAGE:158==
134
Appendix B. WAMIT Convergence Analysis
(a) Coarser mesh reﬁnement with 2496 panels.
(b) Finer mesh reﬁnement with 5382 panels.
Figure B.1: The two diﬀerent ﬂoater discretizations investigated in the WAMIT convergence
analysis. Mesh in (b) is in compliance with [26].
in which x, y and z indicate the coordinates and the subscripts indicate two opposite
diagonal panel vertices.
On Figure B.2 the WAMIT convergence analysis in surge, heave and pitch is shown for
two cases, where the minimum period of 2.475 s has been utilized (NPAN is the # panels).
A ﬁne mesh consisting of 46 panels on the column circumference and column vertical
along with 16 panels radially outwards on the column end disk and heave plate yields a
total of 5382 panels. This mesh has a diagonal panel length of 1.5763 m which is smaller
than the limit of 1.5935 m, thus indeed complying with the regulation in DNV-RP-C205. A
relatively coarser mesh consisting of 32 panels on the column circumference and column
vertical along with 10 panels radially outwards on the column end disk and heave plate,
yields a total of 2496 panels and diagonal panel length of 2.2638 m and hence does not
comply with the regulation. The two utilized mesh reﬁnements can be seen on Figure
B.1. As observed on Figure B.2 the diﬀerences between the two ﬂoater discretisations
are minimal, and will consequently not result in any signiﬁcant changes when loaded
into QuLAF. Executing the simulation with the ﬁne discretisation made it necessary to
employ the direct solver, resulting in heavy computational eﬀort as previously described.
The coarser mesh on the other hand could be computed using the iterative solver and
was almost 9 times faster from start to ﬁnish. Due to the fact that almost no diﬀerences
between the two discretisations are observed, and the desire to minimize computation
time since many simulations for diﬀerent ﬂoater conﬁgurations are to be conducted, it
was rightfully decided not to comply with DNV-RP-C205.

Appendix C
QuLAF Tuning and Upscaling
The QuLAF numerical model of the USTUTT TripleSpar ﬂoater, initially utilised in this
study, is tuned against a higher ﬁdelity numerical model, which could be e.g. HAWC2
developed by DTU or FAST developed by NREL.
In addition to this, QuLAF is originally developed in scale 1/60, hence the numerical
model must be upscaled to full size, as only full size results are considered in the present
study.
In the following sections both procedures are brieﬂy outlined.
C.1
QuLAF Tuning Against FAST
At present QuLAF has been tuned against FAST. The software suite FAST is a CAE tool
for predicting extreme and fatigue loads, as well as for assessing the coupled dynamic
behaviour and response of land- or oﬀshore based horizontal axis wind turbines. Addi-
tionally, the oﬀshore substructures may be either ﬁxed or ﬂoating entailing applicability
in the present study. In short, FAST is an aero-hydro-servo-elastic simulation tool which
works in the time domain. The software, as the name suggests, couples aerodynamic
models (aero), hydrodynamic models (hydro), control and electrical system (servo) dy-
namic models and structural (elastic) dynamic models, to allow for coupled non-linear
analyses of wind turbines.
The supplied QuLAF model has been tuned by Antonio Jurado against FAST for the
baseline TripleSpar ﬂoating foundation in model scale. From the tuning procedure it
was found that QuLAF needs additional tower stiﬀness and additional restoring stiﬀness
in surge and less restoring stiﬀness in heave and pitch to match the natural frequencies.
Further, additional damping was needed in surge, heave and pitch in order to match the
responses computed by FAST. The results from the tuning procedure can for a regular
wave be seen on Figure C.1, and for an irregular sea state with ﬂuctuating wind forcing
be seen on Figure C.2.
137

C.2
QuLAF Upscaling Procedure
QuLAF is originally developed in scale 1/60. In the present study however, no comparison
to previously conducted model tests with e.g. the TripleSpar ﬂoater are to be conducted.
Consequently, QuLAF is scaled up to full size where it is more straightforward to assess
the computed dynamic response and natural frequencies.
In the works of [30] a complete derivation of the scaling factors concerning wind
turbines was conducted and will thus not be outlined here for brevity. This involved the
Froude scaling of the wind turbine ﬂoater and environmental condition, while the wind
turbine blades were designed to produce the correct scaled thrust curve.
When gravity is assumed to be dominating over the viscous shear stresses, approxi-
mated dynamic similitude is achieved by demanding the same force scale for the force
of gravity and for the resulting force [31]. Introduce the Froude number, Fr, expressing
the ratio between inertial and gravitational forces given as Fr =
V
√gL in which V is a
characteristic velocity, g is the acceleration due to gravity and L is a characteristic length.
Approximated dynamic similitude is then achieved by demanding that the Froude num-
ber in nature is equal to the Froude number in model scale, i.e. FrN = FrM, in which
subscript N denotes nature and subscript M denotes model scale. Introducing the geo-
metric scaling factor as λ =
Lf s
Lsc = 60 where subscript f s denotes full size and sc denotes
scaled, all scales are consequently established since gravity is the dominant force [31].
In Table C.1 the various scaling factors may be consulted, note that ρrel = 1.025 due to
diﬀerences in density between fresh and saline water. The results from the upscaling
procedure can for a regular wave be seen on Figure C.3, and for an irregular sea state
with ﬂuctuating wind be seen on Figure C.4. The full size results are computed using the
upscaled model, while the model scale results are computed using the original model with
the end results subsequently scaled up to full size.

Appendix D
Free Decay Tests and Damping Ratios
As noted by e.g. [32] the damping ratio of a dynamic system can be quite a diﬃcult
quantity to determine. Disregarding modal analysis methods, the damping ratio of an
assumed underdamped system can be determined from a record of the displacement
response. This displacement response is ideally a free decay test, in which the system is
given an initial displacement in the desired DoF only, and then released thus making the
system response subside towards zero with increasing time t.
Measuring the successive peaks of the decaying sinusoidal response, x(t), the loga-
rithmic decrement, δ, may be determined as [32]
δ = 1
n ln
 
x(t)
x(t + nT )
!
(D.1)
in which n is any integer number of successive and positive peaks and T is the period of
oscillation.
Having determined δ the actual damping ratio, ζ, is computed through
ζ =
1
q
1 +
2π
δ
2
(D.2)
Damping ratios for the baseline TripleSpar design have been computed in QuLAF in
surge, heave and pitch. The free decay tests have been conducted by adjusting static
wind and static hydrodynamic forcing in order to obtain the desired initial displacement,
which is of somewhat similar magnitude as the actual observed responses when it is
subjected to the general environmental conditions.
A step load type simulation was
performed, where for the ﬁrst 1/3 of the simulation no forcing was applied, in the next
1/3 static forcing was applied and the system was allowed to reach equilibrium, before
the free decay test was initiated in the last 1/3 of the simulation. Please note that it was
attempted to displace the ﬂoater in e.g. surge without inducing motions in pitch, but due
to couplings a small displacement was inevitably observed here as well. Heave is on the
other hand completely decoupled.
The decay tests in surge, heave and pitch are visualised on Figure D.1, D.2 and D.3
respectively. The red signal shows the step-wise displacement as described earlier and
143

144

the blue signal shows the decay envelope given as Ae−ζωnt in which A is the initial
displacement and ωn is the angular natural frequency. The resulting damping ratios for
the baseline TripleSpar ﬂoater are listed in Table D.1 for each DoF.

Appendix E
Sensitivity Analysis RAOs and
Hydrodynamic Properties
Due to the linear basis of the QuLAF numerical model employed in this study, as well
as the linear WAMIT hydrodynamic database, a transfer function commonly denoted as
the response amplitude operator (displacement RAO) may be computed. This entity can
be used to e.g. predict how the investigated ﬂoater will respond to waves in a given sea
state.
When varying the input parameters in the sensitivity analysis for the TripleSpar
ﬂoater, they either directly as in the case of e.g. column diameter, or indirectly as in the
case of e.g. ballast mass, entail the re-computation of the hydrodynamic properties in
WAMIT. Naturally, for the ﬁrst case the diameter (in fact also the draught) changes while
in the latter case the draught changes. Once the new hydrodynamic database, i.e. the
hydrodynamic added mass, radiation damping and complex excitation forces, has been
computed by WAMIT, the RAOs in each DoF may subsequently be evaluated. WAMIT
computes as default the excitation forces per unit amplitude wave, and the RAOs may
thus be computed by rewriting the EoM as [19]
RAO(ω) = ξ(ω)
a
=
X(ω)
−ω2(M + A(ω)) + iωB(ω) + C
(E.1)
in which ξ(ω) is the ﬂoater response, a is the wave amplitude which is unity in this case
and X(ω) is the complex linear wave excitation force amplitude.
RAOs have been computed for the lowest and highest value of each investigated input
parameter in the sensitivity analysis, and are shown in subsequent sections within this
appendix. When plotting the RAOs only the absolute value has been considered, since
the phasing is trivial for the present purposes. Additionally, in the following sections
also the hydrodynamic properties computed in WAMIT are shown for the same values of
input parameters. This appendix has thus been subjoined in order to visualise the eﬀects
from the changing input parameter values on the RAOs and hydrodynamic database.
147
==PAGE:172==
148

Appendix F
TetraSpar Inertia Calculation
In this appendix the calculation of the baseline TetraSpar ﬂoater mass moment of inertia
as well as other signiﬁcant parameters are shown. As for the TripleSpar inertia calculation
shown in Appendix A, the computations are divided into various sections for diﬀerent
parts of the ﬂoater. The calculations shown here are for the baseline semisub ﬂoater
as proposed by Stiesdal.
When investigating other conﬁgurations, or when altering
parameters, other scripts have been written to take this into account. These are however
not included for brevity.
Floater Braces
The outer radius of the braces, rbrace, is
rbrace = Dbrace
2
(F.1)
where Dbrace is the outer diameter of the braces’ cross section.
Computing the inner diameter and radius of the braces, Dbrace,in and rbrace,in, is done
by subtracting the thickness of the braces, tbrace, and yields
Dbrace,in = Dbrace −2tbrace
(F.2)
rbrace,in = Dbrace,in
2
(F.3)
The total volume of all braces, Vbraces, having the same diameter and material thickness,
is then computed through
Vbraces =


D2
braceπ
4

brace,inπ

· 3

(F.4)
where dist is the ﬂoater radius. Lastly, the total mass of the braces, Mbraces, is determined
by
Mbraces = Vbracesρsteel
(F.5)
in which ρsteel is the density of steel.

Tapered Centerpiece
The tapered centerpiece is characterised by its diameter and material thickness at the top,
Dtop and ttop, diameter and material thickness at the bottom, Dbot and tbot, as well as its
length Lcenter. The radii at the top and bottom are given as
rtop =
Dtop
2
(F.6)
rbot = Dbot
2
(F.7)
Next, it is possible to compute the change in diameter pr. m along the length of the
centerpiece, as well as the change in thickness through
dzdia =
Dtop −Dbot
Lcenter
(F.8)
dzthick =
ttop −tbot
Lcenter
(F.9)
Creating two vectors, Dvec(z) and tvec(z), containing the incremental change in diameter
and thickness along the length. As a result the volume of the centerpiece as a function of
vertical distance z may be expressed as
Vcenter(z) = Dvec(z)2 π
4 −(Dvec(z) −2tvec(z))2 π
4
(F.10)
with the resulting volume, Vcenter, thus given as
Vcenter =
Z z
0
Vcenter(z) dz
(F.11)
Finally, the mass of the centerpiece, Mcenter, yields
Mcenter = Vcenterρsteel
(F.12)
Cylindrical Tanks
The radius of the each of the three air-ﬁlled tanks located in clusters of three at each
corner of the ﬂoater, rtank, is expressed as
rtank = Dtank
2
(F.13)
where Dtank is the diameter of a single tank.
The inner diameter of the tanks, Dtank,in, is expressed by
Dtank,in = Dtank −2ttank
(F.14)
in which ttank is the material thickness of the tanks. Consequently, the inner radius,
rtank,in, is
rtank,in = Dtank,in
2
(F.15)
==PAGE:187==
163
The volume of one of the tanks may thus be computed through
V1,tank =

(F.16)
The mass of one tank, M1,tank, is
M1,tank = V1,tankρsteel
(F.17)
Correspondingly, the total volume of the nine tanks, Vtanks, and total mass, Mtanks, are
Vtanks = 9V1,tank
(F.18)
Mtanks = Vtanksρsteel
(F.19)
The top and bottom of each tank are capped by hollow hemispheres. The volume of one
of these hemispheres is computed as

Correspondingly, the total volume of the 18 hemispheres, Vhemisp, as well as total mass,

Vhemisp = 18V1,hemisp
(F.22)
Mhemisp = Vhemispρsteel
(F.23)
Various Parameters
Given a ballast mass, Mbal and a total mass of the attached wind turbine, MW T G, the mass
of the ﬂoater, mf , and displaced volume (based on Archimedes’ principle), Vdisp, can be
computed from
mf = Mbraces + Mcenter + Mtanks + Mhemisp + Mbal
(F.24)
Vdisp =
mf + MW T G
ρw
(F.25)
in which ρw is the density of water. Note that the mass of the wind turbine is not included
directly in the total mass of the ﬂoater, but merely added to the mass when computing
the displaced volume. This is done due to subsequent inertia calculations, which should
only include the actual mass of the substructure itself.
As outlined in the main report, due to the complex geometry of the TetraSpar, the
displaced volume has been computed for a range of draught values, and saved in a table.
Next, knowing the displaced volume from eq. F.25, this is run through the look-up table
until a match is found, and the corresponding value of draught is extracted. The following
==PAGE:188==

Inertia Calculations
Initially, the distance from the centre of the ﬂoater, to the centre of each of the three
clusters (composed of three tanks), in the x-direction, sx, and in the y-direction, sy, are
determined as
sx = sin(30)dist
(F.42)
sy = cos(30)dist
(F.43)
Centerpiece
The mass moment of inertia of the centerpiece can be computed as a hollow truncated
cone. No direct formula for computing the inertia of a hollow truncated cone is readily
available. The inertia may thus be computed by considering several cones, and subtracting
and adding these cones appropriately. Thisisapplicable sinceforeach ofthesegeometries,
the centerlines are coincident. For clariﬁcation the centerpiece is divided into four cones;
where initially, an outer (index 1) and inner cone (index 2) is established, next these cones
are divided into an upper and a lower cone as well.
The volume of the top outer cone, Vtop,1, and mass are

Appendix G
RAOs and Hydrodynamic Properties
for the TetraSpar System Identiﬁcation
As for the TripleSpar sensitivity analysis, the RAOs from QuLAF and the WAMIT hydro-
dynamic properties have been included in this appendix for the lowest and highest value
of the ballast mass diﬀerence and ballast depth, when performing the system identiﬁca-
tion of the TetraSpar ﬂoater. The procedure is similar to that in Appendix E, and will
hence not be explained further here for brevity.
Results from both the semisub-type as well as the spar-type TetraSpar ﬂoater have
been included. Note that for the semisub-type ﬂoater, results have been divided into two
subsections; one subsection for when the buoyancy tanks pierce the waterline, and one
subsection where they do not.
